RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A special game for special people; this is not a game that will appeal to many as it breaks many standard euro-game tropes but still dresses in euro-game clothing; working with others is good, investing in infrastructure is a mixed bag (and doing so early, contrary to many euros, is less advisable). It can be a fragile game at times (especially at lower player counts), and I think that last point is what makes it have a hit and miss with groups, and ultimately cause the game to fall flat at times. At higher counts, it's less fragile, but overstays it's welcome if it lasts more than about 100 minutes. At that point the iterations needed to push stuff through the system is almost too time consuming for the questions the game asks. I can see where the full game of 20 cubes at 5p allows for you to really flesh out who is after what cubes and manipulate that, but it's almost too long for that where as the short game never stretches into that territory. To be fair, experience shortens that time span, and quick play helps even further.

The value here is not necessarily action efficiency, but opportunity and operating in the margins where things are profitable and there is a niche to be filled (or more importantly, *will exist at some time* soon). Where there is low supply, there is an opportunity for profit. That you can win with hardly any containers on the island is an interesting twist (although, harder to do if you are using the expansion), and how the economy grows and contracts in macro is fascinating to ponder, especially crashes when people suddenly don't care about buying stuff anymore and valuation plummets. The cash movement can create epic swings (e.g. when do you starve the economy through infrastructure improvements or higher bids for containers being two that come to mind), especially at lower playing counts. Bottoming out isn't necessarily bad, but it can be atrocious if you're not careful. Riding that wave is exhilarating in lots of ways, and is really what makes the game shine. Needs multiple plays to properly evaluate it and I'd warn anyone from forming opinions on the first couple times. As such, it's *much* better with experienced players, but I only have a return rate of about 2/3rds for a second game.

RATING: 8.101
A very clever economic game. The crucial fact to remember is that there is no bank - money only comes into the game economy from selling goods to the island. If you don't see much goods being shipped you *must* lower your prices otherwise the other players simply will not be able to afford to buy, and the prices for goods ripple through the game. People who complain about "multiplayer solitaire" games should definitely try this one. The designers have done very very well to come up with such a balanced and cogent economic game.

Although I love the game I decided to trade it away since it just wasn't getting enough love. There is always something quicker to play.

RATING: 5
Dry, dry, dry.  I sorely wish they had tacked on a different or improved theme.  This thing simply screams "dry."  What could be more fun than shipping metal boxes?  Heck, they could have gone with almost any other theme and I would be happier.  Shipping corpses for medical experiments.  Shipping puppies for mass slaughter by insane mad scientists.  Shipping rainbows for presentation by Care Bears at art exhibits.  I really don't care, just [i]something[/i] else.

The economic engine is somewhat interesting.  You have very limited control over the consumption side since you can't consume your own goods.  This is good and bad, I suppose.  Certainly a departure from other models.

There are relatively few avenues or strategies to pursue.  You can go for factory production, shipping and warehouses or just the auctions.  Fair bit of player interaction via availability of goods, interrelation of the different engine phases and the auctions.

Really this one just didn't impress me.  It felt dry and pretty boring.  I would play it again but it might require some coercion.  My rating would probably be higher if it had a different theme.

RATING: 10
Economy in a box! As an economic game, it's the best I've ever played: realistic portrayal of the way an economy works with miraculously simple rules create an excellent game. There are all kinds of strategies one can employ, and a player must be quick to know when to change strategies....the economic situation is that fluid. You simply can't play this like any other game, since ruthless competition MUST be tempered by the fact that your opponents are also your customers and suppliers. Quite tense, and (in most cases) it is unclear until the very end who has actually won: looks can be deceiving! Thematically, Container has a very realistic feel to it, and [I originally come from a city with heavy container ship traffic: Halifax, Nova Scotia] the ships and the rest of the game have the RIGHT gritty and grimy feel. The only negative drawbacks production-wise are that the realistic colours are TOO realistic (hard to tell some of them apart); and that I have yet to hear of any copy of Container (including my own) whose player boards are not warped. 
Play-wise, the only mild complaint that I have is that the conditions triggering game end aren't that great. But who cares? It's a glorious game otherwise!

RATING: 9
3-5(5)

Okay, I played this with, um, a full boat of five players and it's very, very good. Right up my alley.

Of course, it's opaque to many players, especially with the instinct, as in most games, to simply think of immediate, short-term gain. Charging high rates for their factory goods, or their warehouse goods, or whatever. And, of course, as with any auction out there, in the world, not knowing how much to bid on the delivery.

If the economy doesn't crash (and I warn against crashing the economy) then it's just a really interesting ebb and flow of goods, as some players realize they need a lot of orange in the market, say, or that they can get away with gouging on white a bit, or something. And then, of course, that island points don't have to matter. Sure, they're great, but if you're making the island deliveries, you might be splitting the profits risklessly (and with a subsidy!)

And that's the thing, every element of the chain has its own influence on the greater economy. Factories are expensive (and goods sell most cheaply), and there are some restrictions, but they solely determine supply of goods. Warehousing goods acts to filter the supply, at a slightly higher margin. And, of course, shippers have least control over what hits market, and it's the slowest of all, but the margins can (and perhaps often are) amazing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
The idea of having to sell your goods to other players feels as though it has great potential to generate an interesting market and a high level of player interaction.  Sadly, Container botches it.  Price your goods higher than other players and watch them gather dust.  The scarcity of a colour doesn't allow you to raise its price because everyone knows they can get just as good a price on the island for any other colours.  The profits on factory and harbour sales are slight and the only real money is to be made from delivering goods to the island.  Hence, the whole production and warehousing part of the game becomes formulaic and constrained by group think.  Like others, I'm unimpressed by the bizarre discard the colour you have the most of end of game rule.  It must be there to fix something but it feels very artificial indeed.

I don't mind the drab, industrial colours of the player boards, but the island looks more like some deserted rock off the coast of Scotland, complete with spatters of bird shit,  than a thriving market for goods. Board warping was an irritation with one of the copies I've played with.

RATING: 8
Container is a distinctive, quirky and amusing game. In Container, the buying and selling of resources must go through several stages, and that makes the game an amusing head scratcher. In particular, the resource cubes you produce from your factory MUST be sold to the other players (at a price you set), who then immediately put them in warehouses on their docks for sale, at prices they set.

These warehoused resource cubes MUST then be sold to someone else in the game for placement on that other player's ship, which then transports them to an island where the shipping player sells them one final time to the highest bidder through blind bidding. The highest bidder then pays the shipper, and places the resource cubes (thrice now bought and sold) in that player's small "destination area" on the island...for eventual, end game scoring.

Twists in the game include dealing out, at the beginning of the game, secret, individualized, "destination area" valuations for each of the resources, and a choice (thank goodness!) on the final blind bidding auction. The shipping seller has the option of taking the high bid from the high bidder PLUS an equal amount of bonus money from the back (a very attractive double payment!) or paying the high bid himself to the bank, and then putting the cubes in his/her destination area, for end game scoring (actually income for each particular cube in that person's "destination area." The person with the most money at the end wins.

On the downside...at times on the first play, Container felt broken, prone to maliciously ignoring players goods for sale (regardless of price), and hopelessly opaque due to the many layers that MUST take place in the game...before a cube reaches its destination where, presumably(?) the game deciding effects take place?

By the end of the first game, I was left wondering which of the hundreds of little transactions in the game were, or were not, decisive in determining the outcome of the game. This game probably comes into much greater focus after a few plays...which is a good thing in a game really.

All in all a very nice game, at least for gamers.

RATING: 8
A strong economic game.  Both micro- and macroeconomics play a significant role.  Pricing decisions are key, but so is keeping the overall economy flush with enough cash (at the right times) to buy your goods.  Underneath the surface this is really a game about tempo and player incentives, but this is not necessarily clear from the outset.

Suffers from similar problems as Modern Art: weak players can destroy the economy and/or throw the game.  It is almost never advisable to buy your own goods, but since it is an option, many players are tempted.

RATING: N/A
Just read the rules and it is pretty interesting.  The rules are clean and streamlined.  I found no ambiguities and no forgotten situations.  The game sounds pretty meh at the moment though, since I can't really visualize the game play.  Well, I can't find the exciting part or times to have clever moves.  I guess the heart and soul of the game is setting the right prices for the containers and valuing each shipment that goes to the island properly.  So in essence it's a game of glorified market stands, which you are the producer and seller and buyer at the same time.  It seems to me though that it would really suck to be the player that has the same initial color machine that someone just made in their island .  This increases supply so your potential profit for others creating your initial color container just plummeted relative to other players' containers.  I am wondering if the game goes on long enough (as far as turns go) that you can recover from poor pricing.  I guess I get now how the more players you have the more chaotic the game gets and with less players the more strategic it becomes.  With too many players, it doesn't give players enough chances to react to the market.  The rule that you dump your most single quantity color container is very interesting.  It forces everyone to get each color container and even have the most of the least profitable container so that they don't lose too much money for dumping.  Actually writing all of this has focused my thoughts and think it is a pretty good game just from the rules.  Looking forward to this.  I am not sure if I will preorder the game or not yet though.  I'll vacillate over this and hopefully make a decision before the end of the month.

After much reading, rereading the rules and seeing the reactions people have about this game, I will "Try Before Buy."  I am even leaning on "Do Not Buy" due to its fragility.  The game tries to employ laissez-faire but it just doesn't work, so I hear.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First impressions were quite favorable, it really felt like a breakthrough in economic games.  Here the players were organically creating the economy, controlling and pricing nearly every aspect of production and retail.  It certainly blew my mind at the time.

As the number of plays grew I noticed that my initial infatuation had diminished.  The game is not short and the game doesn't have a strong narrative.  The beginning and end of the game are not much different, the number of goods from a player's factory store that you buy at once merely increased as the game goes on.

This is not a killer for me as the organic economy idea is still extremely novel.  Unfortunately, by putting so much in the hands of the players, the game is very susceptible to group think.  Factory goods are never placed for more than 2 or 3, harbor goods for 3 or 4.  As such the profit margins are almost entirely in shipping given a normal bid is about 3 a container.

Now, ideally people would realize this and more people would move into shipping and those still in production and retail could raise prices.  This, sadly, never happens and it makes our games play out very similarly.  I sometimes mention this (subtly, I am already known as the overbearing know-it-all) after our games and the other players agree, but nothing seems to change.  There just appears more pressure to keep prices lower in the factory and harbor store and no similar disincentives in the shipping auction.

So the idea of Container appeals a lot more than actually playing it at this point.

Container is a pretty unforgiving economic simulation.  Just like a "real" economy, public sentiment can drive the economy just as much as prices and availability do. RATING: 6
Juego económico de producción de mercancías, compra-venta y después entrega en una isla.  Cada jugador, puede producir tantas mercancías como fábricas tenga, a las que se les asigna un precio de venta.Los otros jugadores, pueden comprar esas mercancías y ponerlas a la venta (con mejor precio normalmente) en  los puertos, donde pueden acudir los barcos a cargarlas para llevarlas a la isla. En la isla, cuando llega un barco, se realiza una subasta oculta y el vendedor puede optar por venderlo al mejor postor (y recibir un incentivo de la banca) o pagar el mismo la mejor puja y quedarse los contairnes para él. 
La mecánica es atractiva, pero por la única partida jugada y comentarios leídos, el juego no está bien diseñado. Se pueden dar situaciones de hundimiento del mercado que echan por tierra toda la partida. Esto hace que le de una puntuación bastante baja.

RATING: 4.5
I SO wanted to like this game!  The game's not that complicated -- you manufacture goods and put them in your manufacturing storage, and then you have warehouse storage at your docks where goods can be loaded onto ships, and then you have your ship which needs to take goods from docks to a central island where they'll be auctioned. The part that had all of us asking, "Really? Are you sure?" is that you can't just move your goods from your manufacturing facility to your docks or from your docks to your ship. Clearly, that would be crazy! Instead, you have to hope that other people manufacture stuff and put it on sale at a reasonable price at their manufacturing facility. Then you have to hope that other people buy the manufactured products and put them on sale at a reasonable price at their docks so you can take your ship over and load stuff up. Um... okay... I admit that it wouldn't be much of a game if you could do the logical thing, but it didn't feel like much of a game anyway.  I normally love dry games, but this one left me feeling like I'd eaten sawdust.

RATING: 9
This one is a bit counter-intuitive and a bit frustrating since you'll never really feel like you have complete control over what you're doing (e.g. you can't force players to buy your stock, you have to entice them with competitive prices) but it is a refreshing change from the same tired bidding/trading/sale mechanics that bog down some eurogames.

Essentially you head a company that handles everything from producing goods to shipping them to their final destination. The way the buying/selling mechanics work reflect how there is a markup on price as the containers get closer to reaching their final destination.

Also, the boats are awesome!

NOTE: [b]This is NOT a game for color-blind people![/b] Even those without vision problems can have a hard time distinguishing the different container colors in bad lighting.

RATING: 9
UPDATE: The expansion recommends taking out one container of each color per player for a shorter version of the game. To me, this seemed perfect to produce a "normal" game, and I highly recommend it. 
---
UPDATE: Just for the record, for me this game is a personal '10'; I think it's great. But I also can't deny that there are a couple points of instability, as noted below, so in the interests of objectivity I'll keep my rating at a 9.
---
UPDATE: I've been asked how "fragile" container really is, and it's a tricky question. Players certainly can cause problems. The #1 problem is for an individual player to over-invest in infrastructure, get stuck with $20 in debt, and be out of it. But that's a pretty easy trap to avoid if you make any effort at all. The second problem is if everyone invests in factories early, siphoning a bunch of money out of the game and stalling things out. Again, pretty easy to avoid if you're thinking about things at all, and If even one player expands their warehouse capacity, though, this should not be an issue. The third problem would actually be substantial *under*-investment in factories. This means fewer goods being produced, and a game that potentially goes too long. Container wants to be about 90 minutes, and most of my games have been, but one game where only one player made significant investment in factories (the factories were 4,2,1,1) went longer than was really comfortable. This seems an easier trap to fall in to.

Anyway, this is a game where you need a good eye for investments, and if the players don't correct for imbalances in the overall economy (by building factories when production is short, or building warehouses when that is the bottleneck), things can go awry. Not badly awry, but it can make the game longer than it ideally should be if some element of the economy becomes a bottleneck and that opportunity is not exploited.

I don't like the "sell a container for $2" beginner rule, because I think it messes up the incentives of the game and can make the game longer, so I've been trying to think of better ways to kick-start the game and ensure that Container comes in at 90min or less. One idea I like is to start everyone with a container in the warehouse priced at $4, in addition to their factory and good. Another option I considered was to give everyone $30 at start instead of $20. Since it's a game of margins more than reinvestment, it seems like the extra $10 at start shouldn't mess things up too badly, and should ensure a fast start.
---
Wonderful game; one of my favorites of 2007, and it was a good year.

I like Container a lot because it's such a subtle game: you need to get a read on what other players are doing and fill undervalued niches. There are legitimate ways to make money in shipping, warehousing, and production, and the game is all about balancing many factors and figuring out who wants what and pricing appropriately. Unlike most economic games, it's not about building infrastructure or figuring out the economic rules; it's more like Modern Art, it's about figuring out where there is money to be made and what the other players are up to. The great thing is that where that is will change as the game goes on as players adjust and adapt. You can't stick with a strategy, you have to be constantly flexible. I also love the fact that money is so tight and margins so low, you really need to manage your money carefully and winning the game relies on consistent good play rather than a couple windfalls. I also like how the game develops and goes through phases of growth and shifting markets, but how it does so organically, with a very clean game system and without any awkward phasing rules.

I've heard some complaints about analysis paralysis, but this really should not be an issue if you play properly (hidden cash, sealed bids).

I suspect that this is a game that may actually appeal more to the broader community of gamers than to the BGG types. I would have few qualms about playing this with interested but non-hobbyist gamers, where I would *never* sucker them with Age of Steam, Power Grid, or Brass. Container is a pretty intuitive game, much more evocative of real economics and much more strongly thematic than the more "gamerly" economic games. And the playing time of 75-90 minutes is perfect.
---
Couple tips:
If you are teaching the game for the first time, make sure you explain to new players that debt is very expensive, and is meant for short-term liquidity, not for long-term investment. Margins in this game are low, and 10% interest is pretty high. New players should avoid debt like the plague unless they absolutely know where income to repay it is coming from within a turn or two.
Also, you should go first, buy a warehouse, and buy a container from another player to kick off the game. This will grease the skids of the economy and ensure a clean start; a common misunderstanding is for players to greatly over-value factories early, which can stall out the game as too much cash is siphoned off. As long as even just one player gets shipping going early everything will be fine, but because Container is different from many currently-popular euros (Power Grid, Age of Steam, Puerto Rico) where it's  impossible or essentially impossible to lose money, its easy to have some misconceptions. In Container, it's not uncommon for shipping or production runs to be minimally or un-profitable. The reference game here should be Modern Art, not Power Grid.
I have some more tips in a general article in the game forum.

Also, some fans of Age of Steam and similar games are playing variants where cash is open and bidding is once-around or continuously-around. Now, I'm not going to tell people how to enjoy games, and some people have a genuine, blanket dislike for hidden cash and sealed bids in *any* context for whatever reason. But playing this way is playing some other game, not Container. Cash is secret and the auctions are blind bid here for extremely good reasons. Having said this, play whichever way you enjoy, but if you're playing with folks who play this way and don't see what the big deal is, it's because important elements of the game have been ripped out.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game is a bit of an odd duck since it seems like there can be really good games and really bad games, depending on how the players play.

Essentially, in Container, players are generating goods into a warehouse which they then sell to someone else.  That player puts those goods into his shipyard, where they are then bought by another player and loaded onto their ship.  That player then sails off to deliver the goods which are auctioned off to the highest bidder.  At that point, the highest bidder keeps those goods for endgame scoring.

So, there are a bunch of levels where players will profit off the goods at various stages of the cycle.  Players set their own prices throughout the process, so there's a lot of undercutting going on, as cash flow is key to the game.  However, undercutting too much just allows other players to get stuff for dirt cheap, so there's a lot of group think that ends up going into the pricing schemes.

Since everything in this game is up to the players, from the availability of goods to the pricing of them, to who you decide to do your buying from, the game has potential to get pretty ruthless.  It's very possible to get yourself into a situation of being low on cash, and find that everyone just abandons you to watch you drown in debt.  This really makes it a game that isn't going to be fun for everyone, as essentially being out of the game could kind of stink.

However, I kind of enjoyed it for what it was.  It almost seems like a social/market experiment as much as it is a game, providing the framework for the various market interactions without any game mechanisms to drive it.  Depending on the players, this could either go well or be a disaster, so it's definitely a game that needs to be tried to see if it works for you.

RATING: 9
This game does not get the love that it truly deserves.  This is a very tight economic game, but unlike some economic games, it does not require an "engine" as your profit is not self-driven, but driven by what everyone else does.  There is a very tight balance between "spending" money to build an infrastructure, but "saving" money to be able to buy the large "Foreign Island" purchases.  Then, of course, the ability to outbid your opponents for your own shipment is an occassionally "coup de grace" that can have an immense swing in the game (either to one's benefit, or complete detriment).

A very well done game that one must dedicate a decent amount of time to playing, but can be an ultimately rewarding excercise.

RATING: 6
Let's start this rating with an "8." I had heard that the economic system in this game is "fragile," and I can see where extreme choices could cause some wonky results. Still, I really enjoyed this first play. Every turn, a player takes two actions, and there's essentially four choices. The rules are pretty straightforward, but the brain starts to hurt when you start to figure out how to get those containers you want from production to storage to shipment to arrival. You can't do it yourself; you need to incentivize others to help you.

A group of people probably need to play this game a couple of times to learn how to value diffferent things. And, none of us priced any containers on the higher end. I'd be curious to see what happens when things get expensive! This is worth trying some more.

Update: more than a year went by since I last played this. And, the game still strikes me as one of the more unique economic games around. A highlight of the game is the way it forces interdependence between players. Those simultaneous blind bids for shipments are pretty critical, and the player that valuates those best will likely come out ahead. The only knock for me is play length, and the endgame condition kinda causes the game to conclude with a whimper. Still, it's a unique game that's great to play once in a while.

Update: OK, so I played this twice, and each time I enjoyed the game... mostly. However, upon reflection, twice both games seemed to drag a little too long, and the game ended with a bit too much of a whimper. The economic system is really slick, but I think I admire the game more than I enjoy playing it. This is another one of those games where I'll be happy to play someone else's copy, but I don't need to own this.

RATING: 10
Update (8/21/09): After two more games with five players, this time with open money, I bumped this up to a 10. I don't know if I'd play any other way now. The decisions got tougher with more information. With open money you're playing the other players, not trying to get the most cash. It opens up a whole new pallette of choices in an already amazingly rich game. I am still always struck by how a limited set of options each turn can have such disparate consequences. The butterfly effects are a marvel to behold. And with open money you actually get to watch them with greater understanding.

Old comments: Midway through my first game, I turned to the guys sitting next to me (not in our game, a CCGer) and blurted, "I am deeply in love with this game." That is a good sign. And I am still in love. I see it going to a 10 soon.

I love the way the game flows. The dynamics of the player interaction make it a constantly shifting challenge. And it is beautiful. I can't stop thinking about it. Another good sign.

RATING: 9
This is a hard game.  Little is obvious.  Most of it is sympathetic rather than directly causal.  Pleasantly unstable, very emergent, more tactical than strategic, but 2-3 turn look-ahead is still required.

Considerably better than I expected.

The core pattern is determining which market segment in the game ie being underserved either through insufficient production or pricing and then occupying that slot profitably.  The game then iterates on that specific.  Ensuring that your container collection on the island is scoring optimised is surprisingly difficult as it relies on incentivising emergent player collusion.  There are many slightly underhandedly sympathetic tricks in this space.  Delightful.  

Don't be scared of loans.  Realise that they are simply sources of temporary liquidity and treat them as such.

Note: Actions are not the most valuable resource.  Really.  Opportunity is the primary resource constraint. 

Update: We replaced with blind bidding for containers with an open auction.  Loans may be taken in turn order before the auction starts, raise or or pass, pass == out of auction, highest bid wins.  The resulting auctions are only marginally slower than the blind bid auctions, but more importantly the winning bid values are much more regular and thus the result auctions are far more interesting.    Highly recommended.  Rating left at 8.5 with open auctions.  Rating with blind bidding drops to 7.0.

Update: We replaced the open auction with a once-around.  As Chris Farrell said, the open auction lost too much information about the player's wants.  Much improved.  Rating now at 8.5 with once-around.  Rating with open auctions: 7.5.  Rating with blind bidding: 7.0.  

That all said this may yet prove to be the one game that I think is actually worth playing with bind bidding.  There's a layer of important signalling that is lost even with once-around that is preserved with blind bids.

Update: Finally back to blind bidding.  The last dozen and a half games have been played with the traditional blind bidding.  I was convinced by Chris Farrell's arguments around the information content of bids to go back to blind bidding and, well, he's right.  The informational signalling in the blind bidding is critical to the design.  However i strongly disagree with his contentions on closed money, only play with open money and will not consider playing otherwise.  If you insist on playing with closed money then I'll quite happily pull out pencil and paper and broadcast each player's holdings every turn until y'all see sense.

Update: Continues to grow on me.  Far more subtle and yet controllable than I'd suspected.  Really quite an amazing design.  Currently rated at 8.5.  There's good potential of a 9.0 rating at this point.  I'm not sure why I'm holding back.

RATING: 8
Stunning game of supply and demand that only improves as players become more familiar with its rhythms. Very susceptible to groupthink, which I consider one of the game's strongest features. This is not a game to play with a group that plays a serious 'metagame.' This sadistic game encourages you to put yourself in a weak position, and then turns around and makes it easy and profitable for your opponents to abuse you. The blind bidding, like everything else in the design, is excellent and vicious.

Physical production quality is a mixed bag. Playing with "secret" money really just means you're wasting everyone's time, so the cards, while pretty, aren't very practical. Poker chips or even Puerto Rico-like money are the way to go. The game's six small boards warp so seriously they put Railroad Tycoon to shame. The wooden blocks are mostly OK, but a few of them are chipped or only half-width, &c. The ships are wonderful. The graphic design is excellent, and I very much appreciate the risky art even when it's not entirely successful. We did not have problems with the colors of the components, but I suppose I can understand those who do.

RATING: 9
I love everything about the economy.. the booms and busts... the niches players try to corner... the collusion... it just seems like such a simple, elegant, and clever system.  This is the first game of its kind I've ever played; where the economic system is totally in the hand of the players.  It is eye opening to watch one player spiral the system down into a depression.  This game has a strong element of timing in it... you want to make sure those that want your goods are flush with cash when you are selling to maximize your profits.  The ebbs and flows of cash flow in this game is an amazing thing to behold.  Picking up on the early auction signaling is one key to maximizing late game shipment windfalls.  This game is groupthink personified, and I love it.

RATING: 5
Container is a multi-faceted economic game where players can produce containers, sell containers to ships from their warehouses, and ship containers to the central island, all of which can provide income. The goal of the game is to have a collection of containers that are valuable to you, which is determined by a randomly given out point value card at the start of the game and remains hidden from the other players for the duration of the game.

I really was looking forward to playing this, and I found it disappointing in practice. The mathematics all boil down to small scale nickel and diming action, and the auctions for the containers at the island tend to boil down to the person whom they are worth the most to bidding a higher amount, and logically winning it. The "most containers are discarded" rule tends to muddy the waters to some extent, but I still found this to be an issue. I found the overall feel of the game to be on the repetitive side as well, not much difference from round to round.

I can understand how groups could greatly affect the way the game plays: i.e.: collusion between factory owners to price up, collusion between warehouse holders to price up...but in groups that refuse to all understand the fundamentals of how the pricing system works, things seem to break down and the only profit to be made is via shipments to the island, which are already lucrative because of the matching money payout scheme. I would argue that it would be even problematic for one player to be under-pricing relative to the rest of the table at either of the two major container points of purchase (factory / warehouse) because then their left hand neighbour would benefit disproportionately from their bad play (if they were smart enough to notice at least) and the other players would meanwhile be sinking in a relative sense...thus, the entire group has to be on the same page with pricing structures, and if they aren't, one maverick seems as though they could throw the game. Maybe if I, and at least 2 other friends liked this game enough we could play it regularly and start to create more interesting play patterns, but as an occasional play, this game seems to create very dull patterns of price increment creep.

I think there is the basis of a really good game here...if the container value was some sort of player controlled market, or something along those lines I think the game would gain measurably. I also think the blind sell at the island could use some sort of patch as well, but that would probably go along with the different way to value the containers. As it stands, I find the game a disappointment. Not bad, not below average, but not nearly as exciting as I felt it could be.

RATING: 6
Ouch, further two points drop. Played the game after a long pause and realized that I do not enjoy it much (despite winning this particular game). I like games with negotiations or 'talkative' interaction, e.g. Modern Art or Game of Thrones. Container is all about interdependence, which is a sort of interaction, but it feels dry and boring. Second, I also like games where I can plan ahead and build my little 'world', e.g. Agricola. You cannot build your own thing in Container or plan ahead much. Maybe you could if all persons involved did understand the game very well and made always rational decisions. However, this has never happend in my experience. 

I still appreciate the game as a simulation of economics.

-----
After 6 more plays, I am knocking a point off. The game encourages analysis(-paralysis). This in turn results in too much downtime in a 4-5 player game. You cannot think ahead because the optimal move usually depends on other players' actions. A 3 player game is not as good for another reason: more players are needed to create a dynamic supply and demand,

RATING: 7
Interesting abstract economic game in which players create and sell goods to one another.  The goal is to be the player with the most cash at the end.  This can be achieved in two ways.  First, by operating on margin.  Goods must be priced low enough to sell, but high enough to earn a profit.  Additional end-game cash is given for a user's goods stored on the island.  The value of these is calculated according to a secret scoring card received during game set up.

RATING: N/A
I don't think this game is for me. As a newcomer I have no right to comment on a game which takes several plays at least for players to get a 'feel' for it, let alone work out some strategies—but I'll take a cheap shot just the same. 

No, I'm not going to argue that the game is broken or that there is an overpowered strategy, or whatever. I very much doubt it is, since it is all up to the players to work out how they are going to drive the game. One major part of the game is creating opportunities for others to give you money, and making some cash off of that. It really is okay to lose money on production and warehousing, as long as you make up in the final auction. Loans are scary, but can help you get up to speed easily. Another part is controlling the speed of the game. You can't ship more containers to the island than the financial state of your competitors allows: if you do you're presenting deals too good to be true, and losing money—even if it doesn't seem like you are. It is hard for newcomers to have any idea on how to make these concepts work in practice; and more importantly, make them work without extensive deliberation. (Of course, nothing is what it seems in this game, and I'm willing to speculate that actually presenting deals too good to be true might be beneficial in some way. Again, incentives play an important role—although it may not have been the 'best' way to play the game in a deus ex machina-sense, you could still attempt to profit the most from the type of economy that is created thusly.)

So I'm not going to comment on the game itself as I strongly suspect that I'd just make a fool of myself. Rather I'm focussing the feel of the game. I think Container represents a boundary I'm not willing to cross. Incentives are Good, but I think there's also something as too much of a Good Thing. I find games with simpler incentive management to be sufficiently challenging already; to stack three levels on top of each other makes my head hurt. In addition those other games can be appreciated to some extent by casual players too, thus ensuring that they hit the table more often. Perhaps if time and fellow players permitted I could appreciate the design more (which, I have to admit, is fairly unique), but for now I'm rather afraid I'm too much of a gaming wuss to spend much time with this title. As such I'll leave the game unrated.

RATING: 8.74546
2009-04-15

I love the economic dynamics here with players trying to produce the rarer goods or just generally trying to find their own place in the system.

The fact that money can only enter the system when you sell off a shipment of containers - and no way to make money without spending money - makes being forced out of the game (by virtue of having no capital to invest) a real possibility. 

At the start, I found myself struggling to do actions that would actually benefit me - having so little money that I couldn't afford to invest. Loans seem like a necessary evil and only after borrowing money was I able to 'get my machine going'.

Would love to play again. I feel it's probably all about predicting the way the market will flow and the hidden player-specific container values would hopefully keep it interesting even when all players have played tens of times.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game has an economy that doesn't work in the way that players expect an economy game to function, which is its biggest benefit and its biggest drawback. Players would think that they would develop an economic engine to produce goods, put them in their warehouses, load them on ships, and sell them at the central island. Instead, players produce goods that may be purchased by other players, who will then place them in their own harbours. Players then attempt to entice opponents to buy goods from their harbours, while buying goods from the harbours of their opponents. Then players deliver goods to the central island, but their opponents blind bid on the goods, which will score victory points in the end-game. Players take a major cash penalty for refusing bids and buying their own goods, so it is possible, but discouraged. Compounding these elements is the fact that different colored goods are worth different values for each player. All of these mechanisms can potentially make players feel a lack of control and/or make the gameplay suffer when all players aren't playing optimally. It can instead make for an interesting and refreshing game, if players do play optimally.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Container stands as one of the purest economic games that I have played. Its self-contained market lives and dies based on player decisions, and it can be surprisingly fragile, resulting in atrocious games if individual players perform economy-destroying actions. Luckily, this is easy to avoid if the players are warned about the impact of their decisions, and an experienced player or two can guide the game back to its proper moorings. 

When experienced players comes together, Container is a thing of beauty as the players try to take advantage of the rhythm of the game, timing actions within the overall flow of the game so that they can gain the maximum advantage and thus vault above their competitors. The game's rules are deceptively simple, but the game itself is fairly opaque. Determining the right move at any particular moment in time can be difficult, and the bidding component only adds to it. Determining the best bid for a particular moment can be maddingly difficult. 

With further play, I find myself at a loss in grasping the implications of various actions and the proper play during a particular situation and thus finding myself playing it less and less. It would probably be easier to play if my opponents were enthusiastic about the game, but it really has a limited following locally. Which is a shame, because I would really like to find out what I am missing.

Then on the alternate turns when I had little money, the goods I really desperately wanted would be put up for auction, and I wouldn't be able to afford them.

RATING: 7
Okay, I gave this a chance, and I really enjoyed it!  There's zero luck in the game, and it's a fascinating simulation of a supply-and-demand economy.  Our first game got off to a pretty quick start, but it lost some tempo about 90 minutes into the game and dragged on a bit. In the end it was three hours long, and everybody agreed it was a little too long but still had fun.

Played this again a few years later and it didn't seem to bog down as much.  Would definitely play again.

RATING: 5
Nice bits, but very dry economic game. I came a poor third, and I have a major gripe. I'll concede that I could have done better if I'd taken loans early in the game, but to me a game with a loan mechanism is a warning to over-spending. In this game, it is a requirement to keep cash fluidity. Certainly a reflection of modern economics, and look where that's got us.

No, my mojor gripe was that as the 4th player, I got screwed. The game basically has three cycles, Production brings in containers, Buying puts them into a dockside, Selling/deliver turns them into cash and VPs. As the 4th player, the most favourable choice of these three was always taken by a player before me. If player 1 produced and set a low price, player 2 or 3 would take them to their docks. If player 2 set a low selling price, player 3 would by them before me. Whatever happened, wherever there was a good deal, I missed out. I could have won more auctions if I had taken a loan. I missed out on several auctions by 1 penny. But even so, the 3 stage flow of the game meant that when it got to my 4th turn, all the good stuff was gone.

So it wasn't a good experience for me. 

However, I just don't think it's much of game. It's not much fun. The whole thing is more of a sim than anything. You cannot vary a previous price unless you spend that action. If I'd played this in playtesting, I'd have been moaning a lot. It's not boring, it's just not fun. Dry economics.

I'll probably still buy a copy though.

RATING: 8.5
This is a very interesting Euro game. It's a bit more complex than most, and I'm intrigued by it. Thematically, I think it's a bit mismatched. You play as the supplier, as wholesaler, as the retailer and as the end customer. The interaction of those parts is a bit quirky and hard to figure out. The victory conditions are brilliantly done, I like auctions as an element of games, and I find this to be very unique. I expect its low rating here on BGG has to do with its complexity, and the fact that a lot of the gameplay is counter-intuitive. I'd love to have a crack at playing it four times in a few days or something, to see if I can get to know it a bit better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
What a great game.  Very subtle.  Definitely benefits from experienced players.  Finding your niche in the economy can be very difficult and is very enjoyable.  I have only once experienced anything close to a recession in this game and that was when everyone went way too crazy investing in infrastructure early on and no one had any money to spend on auctions at the island, but it wasn't that big of a deal, the first couple shipments we just sucked it up and lost a few bucks on the shipment in order to jumpstart the economy.  Now the game is becoming very rewarding.  Games often deviate from the standard $2 factory and $3-4 dock containers as everyone better learns to manipulate the market.  

The biggest variation is in the silent auction.  There can be some luck.  A game can be won or lost there, but prices rather quickly become quite reliable as people learn the value of each spent action.  And if you properly manipulate shipments going to the island, you can tip the scales in your favor to win those auctions, or get paid a lot.  Despite being very subtle, the player interactions and evolution of the economy are just so interesting once you become familiar with the game.

RATING: 9
After what I had read about Container before my first play I expected a deep and rather complex game. Surprisingly, it is neither. I don't even think it is purely a gamers' game. While I guess the economy is a little fragile and can be run into the ground if people play "strangely", the rules are so simple and mostly intuitive that it should be suitable for non-gamers as well, provided they have an interest in the economic theme.

Now, even though I wouldn't call Container a particularly deep game there are heaps of important decisions to be made. As the entire economy is run by the players, pretty much every single action counts. Does that sound contradictory to you? Isn't that awesome?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The economic engine in this game feels forced.  The inability to corner markets sets up a system where there really is no supply and demand.  A few gamey rules create a small sense of this (most containers eliminated, and the 5/10 container)  but the reality is all "real" actions occur on the main island, making the production and port profits negligible.  Pricing slightly high, and without being able to monopolize, means you will always be undersold and ultimately boycotted.  If this happens your ability to make decisions reduces to naught except for wasting actions to reduce costs.  If your warehouses are filled and no one is buying you are seemingly again reduced in your decision process.  There should be ways to actually control the economy more effectively either by forcing a monopoly on goods, or by being able to junk goods if not purchased (possibly selling to bank for minimal gains/losses).  I don't care for the blind values, as this also seems gamey.  In an economic engine wouldn't it make sense that all things would value depending on scarcity. I tend to think that in an economic model there are better ways to generate a truer sense of market values without such obvious gamey mechanics and create values for yourselves. I feel if instead of the most container rule and blind values, something along the lines of differentiating values of containers at end game depending on the amount sold would create a more dynamic market.  For example if at the game end 15 white, 13 tan, 11 black, 9 orange, and 9 brown were resting on the island collectively (total of all players' containers) then the container with the highest number is worth the least and so on. In this case say white is worth 2, tan 4 black 6, and since two are tied both are worth 9 (instead of a full ten). I think something like this would still instigate players to balance their collections but would also lend itself to more thoughtful decisions on container purchases.  I never felt that production had the ability to come to a halt (preventing any tension or goods scarcity) unless people decided they were going to price things above the minimum cost causing people to not buy and forcing the factories to fill up (may sound good in theory but in play it causes the game to shut down for rounds).  This however goes back to the point made before, that as soon as you go anywhere beyond the first two pricing points someone will undercut you, and make the production boards useless. The game also can lend itself to collusion and ultimately force players to their knees (and not in that good screwage/conflict way).  As so much is dependent on players turning the wheels together in synchronicity, any step away from this causes major king making, player bashing, or even worse game lock up scenarios.  Being a perceived leader is doomsday here.  All in all this game just felt incomplete.  It is suggested you need competent players who can value properly, but I feel that any defense of a game by suggesting people play wrong is flawed, unless they just are literally playing the game wrong (as per rules).  A game should not be this fragile, nor should it be so scripted that any deviation in play and strategy can grind the design to a halt. I have more issues but will let them rest for now.

RATING: 8
After 2 plays I can see that this is the kind of game I will like. Simple rules, low fiddliness factor and enough goin on to make it interesting for multiple plays as you learn to wrap your head around the market makes it a winner.   Replayabiilty seems like it will be high.  I wish it were available for play on BSW, it would be perfect for that medium.

RATING: 9
Bought in November 2014.

Everything I've read about [thing=26990][/thing] seemed confirmed by my first plays (5p & 3p). It's an unforgiving economic game, relatively simple rules with fascinating emergent complexity. Awful graphic design, arguably dry theme, but I certainly want to play again.

Update: This has become one of my favourites. I've made a custom set out of Lego. With little pirate minifigs and everything.

Play with expansion rules: Remove one container per player from each colour, allow players to build multiple factories of the same colour, allow loans between players (if a player wants to take a loan and another player is willing to offer it, he must take it or nothing. Another player may offer a higher loan for the same $1/turn interest payment).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
An economic and auction game based on producing and shipping 5 goods distinguishable only by color and each player's unique secret scoring rubric for the different colors. Elegant base mechanics. Unfortunately, the end result is determined more by the trailing players' failures than the leading players' successes. The key to victory in all my sessions has been "hope the other players overbid for your ships full of goods".

This may be fixable with variants (adjusting the bonus paid for accepting a winning auction bid), but I lack the inclination to playtest them since this doesn't seem to me like it would be a truly great game if fixed.

RATING: 5
A near miss. We are fans of punishing business games but the cash felt too tight in this one (in our second game we had all three players go bust -- maxed out on loans and with no money in the market, the game just spiraled into shedding containers for everyone, with no way to get out of the hole). For our third game we played the "beginner's" version, which put more cash into the game and loosened things up considerably, but by the end none of us were especially eager to play again. I didn't feel that the game had a beginning, middle, or end ... at the beginning I was buying cheap crap and selling it for a slight profit, and on the last turn I was doing the very same thing. I wanted to feel like I was building a shipping empire, but instead we were just kind of passing money around in a circle. It is not a bad game, but I think it is subject to going off the rails if you don't play it a "right" way, and while groupthink isn't always a bad thing in an economic game, in this case I think it leads to the illusion of multiple paths when in fact the path to survival (let alone victory) is fairly scripted. Perhaps its greatest sin is that it just isn't as good as Age of Steam, Power Grid, Acquire, or Brass, which means Container is buried pretty deep on the bench and it will be a long time (if ever) before it gets another turn at bat.

RATING: 8
Played 3+ times. This game is prone to group think destroying the fun. It is possible for an individual to get in a spiral of debt and it is possible for a whole group to starve the game of money. Provided there are a few early small shipments then the game should take off fine. I am sure the designer conceived that goods would sell in the mid price range but it is hard for containers to get sold or brought for anything but the lowest costs because someone is bound to undercut and the only way to prevent having to waste an action re-pricing goods is to knock out stuff at the lowest possible price in the first place. I am sure I still have a lot to learn about Container, it certainly fascinates me. I am rating it generously because it is a style of game I enjoy though its fragile nature makes it a poor choice for folk who are not willing to commit to playing it as a group on a number of occasions. *UPDATE* The night after a game of Container I lie in bed thinking about it. This is a good thing. I want to checkout other games by the same designer. I like the way his mind works. Oct 08

RATING: 7
4-player only (experienced players can try a 3-player)

Alterations/House rules: Poker chips in the open, and we have never had problems concealing our bid building from others; I think that there is a beginners game, we skipped it and play the regular.

Touchstones: Blind bidding with ways to gain information about your opponents; Deficit spending; There is a technique for 'seeing' the secret cards of others through the use of spoiling the cargo or gagging the system; Value estimation; Tearing down the sweet system others have built; Game ends at the bottom of one's turn allowing for a last island auction; Avoiding gluts by distancing your objectives from players at either side; Controlling your good colors by pricing high in the early game.

RATING: 6
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
A good game of buying and selling and money optimization. The game itself is pretty simple, but the interactions and dependencies are quite obscure. It is difficult to see what is the best short term play to get maximum long term benefits.

In some ways, it reminded me of a more complex version of Modern Art. You can make money both by buying paintings that go up in value, or selling paintings to others. The same choice underlies much of the play in this game.

It's a good game, but not one that I find myself wanting to play again anytime soon. 

6/4/15 UPDATE: Well, I was true to my word-- second play comes five years later. However, I'll admit I liked it better this time, even though I did much worse. I still see the Modern Art comparison, but now I see how having the added layers (produce, buy, sell, ship, store) makes a richer, more complex relationship. A solid game.

RATING: 8
Very fragile and prone to group-think, but surprisingly accessible.  What I love about this game is how difficult it is to play well.  Manipulating the game in your favor requires creative thinking and long term planning, but players must be willing to be opportunistic when the situation arises, keeping a nice blend of tactics and strategy.

Knowing how the game can break down can be a useful tool to manipulate the game in your favor.  Work on creating an imbalance in liquidity between players and time the ending when the ebb and flow will favor you.  Use opportunistic predatory pricing to gain temporary leverage in grabbing a few extra containers while sinking some money from the economy.  The game isn't won or lost by one clever move, but through a series of inches.

I'm still toying with how to best use loans.

RATING: N/A
straight-forward, but multi-staged buying and selling game. produce goods that you sell in your production area, buy goods from others production areas that go in your port.  sell goods in your port that go on others ships.  buys goods from other ports and put on your ship.  sell goods on your ship to island in blind auction.  buy goods you want from other ships on island in said blind auctions.  only a few actions to choose from.  each action must be such that you profit a bit each step of the way.  There is some memory element if you want to keep track of what everyone seems to value most.

gets a little same-ey after a while.  may want to use fewer containers to help the game go along.  there are some timing issues to consider that adds some interest to the game for me.  also looks as though there are strategies as producers, middle-men and auctioneer.  though in my one game the big pay-offs were n the auctions as people bid up to get the goods.  actually don't mind the blind bidding in this one.  I think it's the best way to do the auctions in this case.

RATING: 10
i've played this game many times, and as others have commented here it is definitely a game that requires folks that interact to create business and grow the economy.  that is precisely what makes this game different than any other it punishes folks who dont like to trade, sell, buy, from their competitors.  As someone else pointed out every player is dependent on the rest of the players, just like a real economy is. 

I have the expansion pack which includes player loans (players can lend to other players instead of using the bank), and I have to say this single feature gives this game a dimension unlike any other.  It is open ended and up to each individual and as a collective to generate economy and try to pull ahead.  

I highly recommend this game as a basic economics course and woudl encourage people to discuss the economic reasons behind their moves following the game.  It is incredible how this game is a microcosm of real world economic activity.  Enjoy!!  :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After my first play, I liked the game, and I think I have a better idea of how it works.  Need more plays to decide if it's great or an analysis-paralysis slugfest.

With low randomness but lots of pricing and auctions, the game clearly depends a lot on who you're playing with.  If people are stingy, then margins will be slim, and money can be very tight.  But if people pay lots for early shipments to the island, then the bank payouts add lots of money to the game, which can be very inflationary.

It's somewhat important to guess what other players are going for, but only to gauge the customer demand so that you can price goods accordingly.  (With less players, this might be more important, because you don't want to create a shipment of goods that nobody wants... including you!) 

The timing of the end game is tricky -- it's a bit of a press-your-luck situation like Ticket To Ride or Power Grid, but not too bad.  It just means that the dynamic is very different from Goa, where the game end is known well in advance.

Rules notes: they're not well laid out.  The Geek has great player aids that make the rules clearer, but the rules about loans are still not clear to me.  For example, I know that I can take a loan at any time, but if I do it to have enough money for an action (e.g. $1 to produce goods from my factories), can I then ship goods to the island for my second action and pay the $10 back from the proceeds of the auction without ever having paid interest?

RATING: 3.5
I really wanted to like this game. It's an economic game, and I'm a big fan there. However, this game functions in no wise like an actual economy. You make goods that you can't utilize. You have to provide incentives to people but you have no definite feedback as to whether or not those incentives are relevant (i.e. you have no idea whether the containers you're providing to the market are the ones your opponents want). Additionally, it's tough to put a price on the goods that you're bidding on once a delivery shows up at the island. Increasing the value of the containers is a key to victory, but it's very hard to keep track of what those goods may have cost to produce or even what they'll be worth to you at the end of the game. In general there is a lack of information that, despite the innovative mechanics, makes this game very unappealing to me.

RATING: 4
An interesting approach to a semi-closed economic game.  You have no direct control over anything other then the price you set for your goods, and what you need to do is entice the other players to pickup and deliver the goods you need on the center island.  Hopefully you will have the cash necessary to bid for their delivery.The components are generally nice, and I especially like the size of the ships.  The color of the ships are so so at best (they can be painted over), and the game cannot be played by color blind people (some colors are difficult to differentiate for those of us who are NOT color blind!)With only two actions to perform when it is your turn to play, downtime appears to be minimal.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Container is a totally neat game of economic player interdependence. Because money entering or leaving the economy is a player-driven choice, players are literally tethered together and are collectively responsible for the health and well-being of the shared economy (as to not bring the game to a grinding halt, although I hear that that's a great strategy for tanking players who have invested too heavily in infrastructure and have no liquid(-able) assets; but also a good strategy to never have new players play it with you ever again!). The game then becomes almost literally one of game theory as presented within economics: it is not only a game of emergent and ever-shifting valuation, but also one of playing the prisoner's dilemna (i.e., how much of a micro-advantage can you squeeze out of a relationship before your pair decides that it is worth more to them to make the wholly selfish and self-serving decision). 

Because this game is primarily and almost strictly an economic one, I wish that there were a bit more space for (like in real life) the intangibles that exceed economics (but I also understand that it might also be against the spirit and intentionality of the game). As such, I'm still not sure if it's a game that would go up or down for me with more plays as I personally prefer more volatility in games I play (over micro-accrual of advantages).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
2-16-10
After a few more plays of Container with other people in both 3 and 4 player games, I find it to be more interesting than I did months ago when I played it one time with 3 players.  The volatility of the economy is really interesting, in that one person throwing a wrench in the system can bring the money flow to its knees.

This can be a blessing and a curse.  On one hand, the push and pull of the money and containers is engaging and interesting when everyone is playing to win.  On the other, if one person 'gives up', or doesn't take out a loan and quits contributing to the economy, things get lopsided and slow.

I also like the hidden island scoring.  All of the factors that go into which containers you're willing to buy at what prices, who you think will buy them at what prices, how badly you need them and the value per container - this can give some good brain burning that you feel good about when done right.  Or as done in our four player game, pretty much ignore the island and just produce and ship crazily to get a huge pile of cash!

I really like the way everything interacts and different ways you can win but it's still a little too dry for a 10, and not a game I think I'll get to the table too often.  Still one of my favorites.

RATING: 9
Not only is this the best economic game I've ever played; it is in fact arguably the only really economic game I've ever played; while other games with that label are really "build a money-making machine," this game captures the positive sum nature of actual markets: Every transaction is a question of "how do I make this valuable enough to the other guy that he takes it, while ensuring it's even more valuable to me?"  Absolutely brilliant.

RATING: 9
Good gravy do I ever lover Container. It's not really a game, it's a social experement, as different groups lead to completely different experiances. You and your fellow gamers are the entirety of an economy, from production to distribution, and you are allowed to price your stuff however you wish (well, almost). Leads to quite a dynamic process, and a lot of thinking about how to improve your financial situation, based on the styles of your fellow business owners. The entertainment level drops when you're not doing so well (like never being able to ship anything because everyone got there first), but it's still an enjoyable time. Plus it's fun to play with the little boats.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First played March 18th, 2009. Taught in ~15min, played in a little over 2hrs for 5 players. This game has a good balance of luck and strategy. I was surprised at how much depth it has for such a simple concept. I'd recommend giving this a game play and consideration in purchase if you can manage to find 2 hours to play the game. Each player starts with $20, a factory of a different type of good, a good from your factory, a warehouse, a player area, a ship, and a goal card. Each players goal card is different and has the 5 types of goods listed. One good is worth 10VPs, 5/10VPs, 6VPs, 4VPs, 2VP. There is a common island where players sell their goods for VPs. On your turn you can, buy a factory($6,9,12; must be for a different good; store 2 more goods), warehouse($4,5,6,7; lets you store one more good), produce 1 good for each factory you have($1 to player on the right; may pick which good type if you have limited storage; set price of goods; reorganize goods), buy goods from another player at their set factory set price(limit to your storage area; place in your storage area/shipyard; set price; reorganize goods), move ship(to another player(can't be yourself) area(free action to buy goods;max 5 on ship), to sea, or island(all other players secret bid $ (to you) on your goods for VPs(for them));if a tie, tied players bid additional $ blind; seller can accept high bid and gets same $ from the bank or pay $ himself) Each player takes two actions and a player may do any action twice except produce. You may also take a loan out or $10. You pay interest of $1 at the begining of each of your turns and then can pay back the loan. The end of the game occurs on the turn of the player that causes two of the goods to run out. At the end of the game, you lose all of the good that you have the most. If there is a tie, you lose the 10/5VP good. If you have at least one of each good, you get 10VP for the 10/5VP good. Then you cash in your money and add up your total $ or VPs. Each good you have in your warehouse is worth $2. Each good you have on your ship is worth $3. Loans -$11.

RATING: 7
I wasn't expecting too much, but was pleasantly surprised.  Pickup and delivery games tend to be dry and mechanical.  Mercifully, that's not true with Cargo.  Enough of the information is hidden to make the game more of a psychological guessing game than a mathematical exercise.  There are a couple of things that hold back a stronger enthusiasm for the game.  First, its a touch long and I can easily imagine it dragging. It seems very hard to increase the value of containers.  Yes, there is an element of group think here, but its perhaps to easy and too tempting to undercut your opponents.  If the supply of goods was as interesting as the auctioning, it would add another layer of depth.

RATING: 10
After one play with three...

Reading the rules gave me a sense of intrigue, hope, and fear.  Fear that this game would not live up to my expectations. Hope that it could exceed them, and intrigue as far as what exactly would transpire.

First, the ruleset is actually quite simple.  It was very each to teach - I had read through the rules once before the game, taught the "outline" version of the rules to the other players, and referred to the book perhaps three times to confirm what we had already figured out.  Not bad.

The game started awkwardly, as we didn't know what to look/plan for, or how to react to other peoples' actions, but this changed slowly through the game as we started figuring out where there was money to be made.  As the light bulbs turned on, the game went from being an exploration of a strange, abstract, opaque game, to understanding enough of how the pieces fit together to push our agendas, even if still a bit randomly.

The end result was a thinky, convoluted, but not overwhelming game that plays lighter than it is, results in more fun banter than far more colorful games, and looks like it should be quite replayable.


Two plays...

This game could be a 9, which, for me, is as good as it gets.  I'll give it a few more plays before I go there.  I came in dead last in the second game because I pushed my "10" containers to being the most common right before the end of the game.

I am starting to see how to subtly control the market, at least in theory... other players have to take your bait!  I am awful at bidding on containers.  I always seem to bid 1-2 less than the winner if I really want something.

Bottom line Container is a fine game, one I want to explore further, besides it is one of the few games my GF rated very high.

RATING: 8
If Sid Sackson had created a hardcore economic game, it would have been something very similar to Container. Much like the great Sackson economic games such as Acquire and Executive Decision  (which are lighter), this game uses a simple rule set to create a self-contained economy that is driven by player decision. I would even say that this is more directly player influenced as players are constantly producing goods for sale, buying and selling to each other, and creating their own market economy in the process. The gaming styles and personalities will heavily influence how tight or loose this economy will play.

Well, kind of. The game definitely tries to keep things on the tight side with scarce money and slow, expensive production and goods movement. It is so tight in fact, that the game almost feels a little long at times, even though the play time isn't long at all. I'm starting this one at an 8 because of this. I love Sackson and I love heavy games. Although 8 is a great score for me, I thought this game had the potential of hitting a 9 or 10 out of the gate. After a few more plays, it may get there, but with a game so heavily dictated not only by player choice, but player personality and one's innate evaluation skills, it will take some time to sort out the weaknesses of players from the weaknesses of the game. Great fun, no matter my score.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Purchased Fall 2007.

The short answer:

Mechanically easy yet tactically rich, Container is consistently great and largely ignored. It is a transcendent micro-economic experience in which players create the economy and determine the cost and value of everything within it. The game system is brutal to those who ignore others and go it alone and often rewards the individual brave enough to pave their own way while keeping tabs on other players' needs. The value others place on commodities can be quite opaque at the outset but a watchful player can start to see purchasing patterns as the game unfolds. 

Container features everything I love about modern games: intense player interaction, emerging partnerships, tactical adjustments, variable sessions, and a unique experience each time out.

The long answer:

It's been mentioned that the game is fragile; I would say it is dynamic. It is true the game provides no protection or insurance against poor play, nor does it feature catch-up mechanics. However, what the game does provide is a foundation upon which players will build and police an economy all their own, an economy whereupon all values are fluid from play-to-play. 

Container is anti-solitaire. Each decision will effect the other players and there is no selfish action to be taken. If you want to produce you have to pay the union boss (player on your right). If you want to buy containers for your harbour you must do so from another player. If you want to buy containers for your ship you must do so from another player's harbour.

Container is dynamic with different paths to victory emerging each session. One session may find a shipper winning, the next someone who specializes in producing or in wharehouses. The person who does the best is often the one who doesn't follow the herd.

Container is brutally realistic. Supply and demand function how they would in a real enviorment. Not paying back your loans can kill you as the bank rightly siezes your assests. No negative VPs in this game (ahem Agricola). If you don't repay, the bank will gets its money.

If you like intense player interaction, fluid markets, variable sessions, and realistic micro-economics, then I can think of no better recommendation than Container.

WIFE RATING/Comments - 7
I like managing the different aspects of the game without feeling overwhelmed. 

*Hall of Fame Designation

RATING: 9
The love-it-or-hate-it reputation kept me away from Container for a while, but no longer. 

Container is a game about manufacturing stuff and selling it to another player who will sell it again to another player who auctions it off to the highest bidder. Sounds kind of ridiculous. In fact, it's a blast. You eke out what margins you can along the way, and only at the end of the chain does profit flow in from outside the game. The system is simple and innovative, and it's neat to see a mostly closed economy with realistic gains. This is not rags to riches. You fight for your modest profits in Container.

On your turn you get two moves. You can produce new goods, purchase a new factory or warehouse, or move your container ship to pick up, transport, or deliver goods. Each player's scoring conditions are different, which means everyone approaches the auctions with different values in mind. It's a simultaneous blind bid, which in this context is a stroke of genius because you can infer a lot about other player's secret scoring goals by their bids on various shipments.

Container pulled me in right from the setup. It is innovative, approachable, smooth, satisfying, and challenging. I would play this with hardcore gamers but also with my parents over the holidays. Few games do this; take note.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
