=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An economic game from my favourite author Franz-Benno Delonge - a perfect fit! And Container didn't disappoint. It incorporates economic concepts in a way that I haven't seen in a game before, e.g. deflation. If players spend too much to the bank the amount of money decreases and causes deflation. Gamewise this can lead to a grinding halt and could provide for a long and not very pleasant game experience. Just as it would be in real life. Another subject is collusion between players which can break the game if players decide to form an alliance to make overpriced bids for each others goods. Also state subsidiaries are modelled in the game to enable inter-company trade. It's not as intense in modelling supply and demand as I had expected from the rules but that might be due to our group consisting of new players not being able to evaluate the game situation good enough and exploiting it.
In sum Container is a unique game with great depth while the rules are astonishingly simple. One of the trademarks of Delonge.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A Puerto Rico-esque game (which I love), but with significant differences that weaken the game significantly:

1. Puerto Rico has the plantations to randomize the game throughout; Container only has random setups (which aren't really that random), plus the chaos introduced by one's opponents. It's a minor difference, but I do not see replayability between good players.

2. The investment decision to reward phase is very long (3+ turns), and highly susceptible to one's opponents. Since one is dependent on one's opponents for economy, a misjudgment on your part (or their part) hurts massively. It's even worse because of the long lead time of decision to benefit. At times, watching the decisions is like watching a train wreck unfolding in slow motion, but being unable to do anything about it.

3. Puerto Rico can suffer from the same weaknesses, but the possible choices are easier to see (e.g. they might Captain, forcing me to ship my coffee), and generally quicker to correct, either with a new strategy, a building, or roll choices. With better players, it's easier to predict likely moves, which helps. It's really hard to tell in Container whether one's decisions are good (or bad), so one's opponents likely have to struggle through the whole game with them, and even if you lost, it's not readily what set of decisions were the major decisions.

RATING: 7
Container was a game that for some reason I was very hesitant about picking up, but once I played I found it to be a refreshingly unique game experience.  

The components are passable.  I like the ships, the containers are fine, though I find the choice of colors (for both containers and player colors) a little strange - why not go for a greater color difference?  I really like the artwork on the cards, box and player boards, though the island itself seems a little weak.  The cards seem a little flimsy and the player boards seem a little warped and of low quality.  I find the game to be overpriced for the quality of components that you receive.

The gameplay is fun and feels different than most economic games, which seem to deal (at least these days) with engine building.  You do have to build up your infrastructure, but the key here is interactive pricing - controlled greed and a good idea of what is more attractive to certain players.  Initially the system seems very clunky, but after a couple turns you realize that it works quite smoothly and seems to simulate supply/demand and other economic principles quite well (for someone like me who knows nothing about economics).

My one problem with how the game plays is that it goes too long - hopefully experience will bring the play time down.

RATING: 6
Like Agricola, I am rating this a 6 after my first play. Unlike Agricola, though, this one has a much lower ceiling. 

-Repetition, repetition, repetition. You are doing the same thing over and over and over. It's a clever market system, and I totally give it credit for that. Specialization pays. There are three things to specialize in, and two of them don't pay all that well unless you can manage volume that's unlikely with fewer than five. So... specialization in shipping pays.

-Lack of variability. The big differences are going to be in rather boring things like "Whoa, you just spent twenty on this bid instead of eighteen!" There isn't a whole lot here to keep my interest.

-Lack of narrative. What I sort of hold against this game a little bit is that you're producing, shipping, producing, shipping, a lot of the moves are fairly rote, and then... someoneboughtthelastbeigepieceHOLYSHIT game over. There's no arc, really.

These are minor complaints given the smoothness of the system. It's a little long but whatever. I don't mind losing, I mind doing the same things over and over and hoping for a different result.

RATING: 9
An astonishing economic game. This pushes all my buttons: easy to teach, memorable rules, sensible gameplay, and elegant mechanics. There are a few wonky elements, thematically, but it's not hard to grasp how this plays. Games play out differently depending on the players at the table. The game is a framework for interacting with other players, and you never feel as though you a playing the game. A brilliant game. 

One complain is that new players can tip the game in the favor of more experienced players without realizing it. Player order can also matter here. Experienced players will often not buy from the person to their left because that only gives them more money on their turn. Inexperienced players won't see this.

I don't consider this a serious problem. The same thing happens in any complex game: the inexperienced players are at a disadvantage and can, through weak player, create opportunities that experienced players exploit. It happens all the time in everything from Agricola to Hansa Teutonica. So, yes, the game is better when everyone at the table has a reasonable level of experience. But to me that's a sign of a game that I want to play because I know I'm playing against other players, through the mechanics, rather than against the mechanics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Okay, the theme is admittedly dry and fairly abstract--you're producing containers (of anonymous color--they're not necessarily symbolic of anything), warehousing them, and/or shipping them to a fantasy island in the middle of your dinner table.  But ironically part of this dryness is what makes the game shine. 

Take the abstract layout as an example: the sea on which you float is your dinner table and the game is completely comfortable acknowledging that fact.  There's a self-referentiality  to it.  Container says, "Hey, it's clear that I'm a game you're playing at your dinner table, so let's bring your dinner table into the mix."

This demand that the game makes on its players is partly a product of being dry--players need to use their imaginations to put themselves in the position of container-shipping robber barons.  

But this demand is not simply a factor of theme, it's an essential part of the gameplay. Because the economy of this game is equally imagined by its players.  Prices are set not according to predetermined rules--a flow chart in a rule book--but by the supply/demand mood set by the players themselves.  Again, Container says, "Listen, yes I'm a game. We all know that. But I'm going to ask that you pretend I'm not a game; pretend instead that I'm a real economy, one that grows organically as money becomes available through smart business practices, and one that can also shrink to death-knell size if inflation and incautious spending run rampant.  You are both the only producers in that economy and the only consumers.  What you do within those roles is up to you, but the one with the most market savvy wins." 

With these demands, dryness suddenly becomes immersive.  When Container clicks, the dinner table truly does become the ocean, and players begin behaving like buyers and sellers on fantasy island, working within an economy they've created for themselves.

RATING: 8
Self contained economy in this game can quickly make someone bankrupt or head into a near death spiral if you aren't careful.  Basically, this game is a three tiered economic structure of making goods, buying/selling goods as the middleman, and likely selling goods as the shipper (although you can choose to pay the high bid and keep your own load, thus taking precious money out of the game!).  It appears that you generally want to pursue one of three strategies (shipper & factory, shipper & warehouse, or factory and warehouse).  Whoever can find the niche market will likely do better.  This game can feel very repetitive as you are often doing the same actions over and over again - this seemed to be the major complaint in our first play although I think future plays might be faster and thus it won't feel such.

RATING: 7

The things I like most about container: how it forces players interactions, how it teaches valuable economic lessons that are also true IRL and finally it's high dynamics to mechanics ratio (to use MDA terminology). 

The more players are familiar with it, the better Container becomes.

Note to newbies: when the money stops flowing, the game slows to a crawl. Also, fulfilling what nobody is doing is often a winning strategy.

RATING: 9
[microbadge=5]

It's staggering how the economy works in this game.  It's definitely not a coöperative game, but you need to work with other players just to make sure that the system doesn't implode on itself.  Constantly while playing, I wonder how on Earth I was going to make my next turn work, only to be saved by some friendly opponents who purchased from my factories or harbors for their own ends, keeping me afloat for just one more turn.  It's crazy to me that any selfish action not only costs you money but removes money from the closed system completely, while the only way for money to actually enter the system is to set up opponents to fulfill victory conditions on their manifests.  This isn't a coöp, but it does walk a fine line between cutthroat and coöperative just to survive.

RATING: 7
Played the base Valley Games version of the game.  The game was very fun.  I found the game to be pretty fun.  You can build factory or warehouse space.  Other actions include buying goods or moving you ship.  If you go to your island, you don't keep your goods, you auction them offer.  Offers from players are matched by the bank or you can match the high bid yourself and keep the goods.   

If people try buying their own containers, the game will break down.  But this does not seem like it would occur often.  

A solid game but it may need expansions.

RATING: 8
First impression after two games (4 & 5p).  An elegant and interesting economics game that unfortunately is too heavy/difficult for my skills.  Definitely a seminal game that every gamer hobbyist should experience at least once.  The rules are so well designed, elegant, and simple, that they stand out of the way, allowing the entire focus to be on the board and the interaction among the players.  My main reluctance is that I don't have the computational power nor the will to climb the steep learning curve and be a competitor in this type of game.  My first two plays have been two very humbling experiences.  I feel that in order to be able to play this game well one has to either crunch the numbers or play it often enough to better understand it, and I don't have the desire/energy to crunch the numbers and don't have enough time to get better at it.  In spite of the above comment, it is so elegant and intriguing that I want to play it again before I pass final judgment.

RATING: 5
The game rules once explained seem pretty intuitive but I noticed players kind of fumble through the first game as they are not really sure what to do next.

I played an incomplete game once at FallCon - the Valley game guys are a great bunch - especially Torben - tons of fun playing with him.  His enthusiasm is great.  

Communication from the Valley games website has been non-existent and that has been annoying. 

My copy arrived through the mail; it has warped boards and was missing the extra bonus stuff I should have recieved for pre-ordering.  The color scheme for the containers is poor - the colors are too similar even with really good light.  I can't imagine the mistakes I would make in low light conditions.  Three shades of orange/tan just doesn't work.

Tried it again with 3 players - not sure if I enjoyed it quite as much as a 5 player game.  

I think it is really important to get a few well timed shipments over to the island to win.  You really need both the gov't donation and your opponents offering to get going because money is so tight.  Once one opponent gets a lot more cash than the rest of the players it might be hard to stem the tide.  The train keeps a rollin for them and grinds to a halt for you.

Getting behind with loans is a sure way to lose.  If you need money, only plan on borrowing for a few turns maximum.  The interest rate is just too high to ignore.

The game needs more plays for me to provide a firm rating... but for now a 6 seems about right...

Played a full game - 5 players - the game went on too long - only three of us were really producing goods.  The game is dryer than I would have liked - it just seemed to lack excitment.  I think shipping is the biggest key to the game.  The cash incentive from the government might be a touch too good.  I overpayed for the last ship on the last turn and perhaps gave the game to my opponent (by only a few dollars)...  If players are bidding 15 or more dollars for a shipment that is at least 30 dollars to the shipper if they take the money... that is a pretty big windfall.  If someone can do that early in the game they probably will have it made.

RATING: 3
I just can't wrap my head around this game. If I hold out hope that there is some rhyme or reason to the arbitrary pricing of the goods during any of the three points of sale, then after two plays I declare it broken. If I instead accept that an equilibrium economy is the natural state of this game and the real meat is in the psychological fake outs of the auctions given the individual players' hidden scoring valuations, then there's not enough information to go on and the other 3/4 of the game is a waste of time.

Most likely the game is using the slightest variance in the economy as information to help determine the hidden valuation of others' so as to maximize one's edge in the auction, but this neither works nor is interesting. I'd say I'm waiting for someone to prove me wrong but honestly I don't think I'd be listening.

RATING: 9
Love this delicately balanced, tightly funded, build, produce, pick-up and deliver to "auction-island" economic game. It's like no other game I've played. The bits and boards are very nice, especially the resin ships; the rules booklet is excellent.

Regarding gameplay: 1) The blind auctions work very well; the decision whether to sell the goods to the highest bidder or buy them yourself is often very difficult; 2) Finessing the total number of containers you own in each color to maximize your endgame score is challenging and fun. Apparently [i]Container[/i] is at its best with four or five players, but I've really enjoyed it with just three. The expansion, "The Second Shipment," is well worth having. [5 plays]

-------

5/21: Update. After a second play I can say that although the above concerns remain, our game was so tight, so intense, and so much fun that the game at very least deserves another half-point ratings bump. I am particularly interested in the extent to which the actions on my turn create situations that encourage or discourage particular actions on my opponents' turns. This is a great game, and I can't wait to play it again.

RATING: 6
Container is definitely an interesting game, perhaps moreso conceptually than in actual play. I got out of sync in my first game and did not react properly, so the game became quite dull as I felt myself lagging further behind. In my second game, I watched another player fall into my same position but respond correctly and ultimately win the game. Unfortunately, that led to me falling out of sync in this second game, though that was more the fault of the player to my right, who was in a position to respond to the former player but did not. I suppose with more experience, I could have pointed this out and gotten him to shift gears, but then again I generally find "advice," particularly in games like this, to be highly suspect: players think they know how to play well, but all they really know is the groupthink they've previously experienced. I think [boardgame=118][/boardgame] provides a similar and more fun experience in far less time, but that's one of my favorites; echoes of it here are actually nice, and I'm interested in messing around with this system some more.

RATING: N/A
This is one I may really have to play before purchase. For 3-5 players. Runs ~120 mins.

You produce goods that are purchased by other players. You set the price and have limits on how many containers you have available to store. Other people ship your stuff to an island and they try to sell it. The end game scoring combines profits with container values in your purchase area; although you throw out the color of container with the highest number of pieces.

It is more of an economics game than anything and cannot be played as multiplayer solitaire. It requires constructive player interaction.

Sounds very, very interesting.

RATING: 8
A very interesting economic game that covers production, sale and shipping of goods.  Although the game appears very complex at first blush (partly due to the way the rules are presented), the mechanics are quite simple.  The unforgiving part is HOW you use the mechanics.  This game will KILL you if you expand too fast, or mis-price your goods, or buy the wrong goods, or . . . . The two most important rules (IMHO) are (1) you can only have 2 loans and (2) you discard the goods you have the most of on the Foriegn Island.  Pay close attention to these rules.

RATING: 9
This has to be one of the finest games ever designed. In a sense, the designer did not create the mechanics--the mechanics are a nearly perfect microcosm of pure economics! What the designer did was to condense actual economics into a playable board game! I introduced this to the table this week for our gaming group. Some of us are gaming fanatics, and some were very casual, almost no game experience at all. And everyone at the table just kept saying all night, "Wow, what an incredible game! How simple, and yet strategically deep." It is all about your skill in valuing the containers. Value them well, and you will win. Value them poorly and you will lose. That simple. 
Updated from 8 to 9.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
In this game, everyone has a player mat, where there are factories for producing goods (five different kinds of goods are available), warehouses to store goods, and players have a ship that can load goods. Each player is dealt a card, outlining the secret values of the goods, which is the worth to them, but not the same as the other players. The actions available to a player are build a factory, produce goods, buy warehouses, buy goods for warehouses, load goods into their ship by buying from someone else's warehouse or auction off goods in the central island. Goods loaded onto the island count for VPs as indicated by theri secret card, and money is equal to VP as well. The trick is that your highest number type of one good is discarded before calculating the score. The game could run a little longer than expected, and could be a bit dry, doing the same actions over and over. I need to get a few more games under my belt to see the different types of strategy.

RATING: 10
[I always play "shorter game" i.e., remove as many containers as the number of players for each color. Then it is ideal 90-minute game]

It is a deceptively simple economic game with emphasis on making marginal profits, not on building infrastructures. It took me 3 games to start appreciating the system. I remember that my 2nd game was awful as everyone went to economical death spiral. However, it is easily avoided and once you know it, it is such a great game. Understanding the scoring system is a key to do correct bidding.

After 8 plays, now it is a 9. It is very very good.

RATING: 10
Secret Santa 2008

Container is in many respects a near perfect economic game - it simulates the whole supply chain from production to warehousing to sales, it features subsidies and loans. It has great depth - very little analysis paralysis and fast turns. However, it's the kind of game you really need to give five or so games to shine, as the rewards are reaped once you master the rules.

RATING: N/A
Truly unique game. People often describe a certain type of games as "economy game", but usually the so called "economy" is a neat system that works according to well defined rules (that is, more like well regulated market).  I think that Container is an economy game whose economic system is flexible, subtle, and unpredictable enough to feel like the real one. The game is extremely open-ended; your playing experience can vary widely depending on how the people around table play collectively.

That being said, I'm not sure whether the realistic market simulation equals a good game. Maybe as an eurogamer I crave for some level of structure in my games. This game is so widely open that it is very hard to tell what to do. I'm pretty sure this is an extreme compliment in some people's book, and I am not criticising the game by any means. It just felt too strange to me...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I like economic games and this is a good one.  The economic model, like many have stated, is a fragile one.  I suggest playing with the Domestic Sale Beginner Variant, as it adds that little bit of cash that keeps things going.  The game starts out a bit clunky and slow, and you must be incredibly careful not to overspend at first.  The last 2/3rds of the game gets very interesting, and there are some tense moments.  Strong bidding element.  The time requirement for this game is a long one, and even though I do enjoy this game, I can't decide if the time commitment is worth it for the fun/time ratio.  I guess I'll find out though, as I'm sure more plays of it are in my future.

RATING: 4
To master this gamer's game, you'll need the skillz of an advanced Puerto Rico and Modern Art player. You are very much playing against the other players in a somewhat scripted milieu, scripted in that it is easy for all players to hit a groove and start performing the same virtuous cycle actions, at the expense of one or two players. Because of its open-endedness, seating matters. In a 5er, if you're sitting two seats away from someone playing the same strategy, with the player in between not playing to that strategy, you will effectively trounce the first player by simply pricing everything a bit more favorably. And if that first player is me, I will then kingmake against you so that a different player wins. So the game suffers from that. Also, it is extraordinarily AP-heavy, way too long, and dry as all hell.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Interesting system. One of the possible actions is to build machines. Machines can make containers with the production action. Offer those containers for other players to buy(another action type), once they are bought they are put in the warehouses(they can be built by a different action) in the buying player's harbor. Other players can buy those containers from the harbor and load them onto their ships. The ships can travel to an island where the containers are auctioned off in a blind auction. Either the highest bidder puts the containers in his area on the island and pays his bid to the auctioneer and the auctioneer gets the same amount additionally from the bank, or the auctioneer pays the highest bid to the bank and gets the containers. The movement of the ship from the open sea to a harbor is an action that includes buying containers, the movement to the island is an action that includes the auction. Moving to the open sea also costs an action. Each round there are two actions available to each player. There are five different kind of containers and their worth is different from player to player (at setup each player gets a card where the worth of their containers are shown). At the end of the game the acquired containers on the island are scored, but each player first discards the kind that they have the most of. To these scores are added the worth of containers left in harbors and on ships and the money acquired during the game. The auction has somewhat of a modern art feel, you don't want to bid too high, because you would benefit the auctioneer more than yourself, but betting too low could mean not winning anything. I think it is a solid game, but I feel that there isn't sufficient control of your own destiny. You can offer incentives to other players to help you, by lowering your prices, but they may choose not to help you, by not buying from you.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Two levels of buying and selling merchandise at variable prices, with little idea of how to value everything. And then a blind auction. This spells a guaranteed failure for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
First impression (4 players): The game is about buying/manipulating goods. Especially about manipulating them. The game might make sense mechanically but thematically it's really not making much sense. First a player must produce goods. Then somebody must buy them to his warehouses where someone else (could be the original producer) will buy them onto his freighter. After that goods can be taken to the island and sold.

Why cannot producing player sell goods straight to the ships? Why a player must buy goods from somebody else to be able to place them to his warehouses and cannot transport his own (cheaper) goods there? Why all the goods must travel between 2-3 players before somebody can buy them?

This isn't game breaking or anything but this moving back and forth just felt a bit silly while playing. A lot of energy (and time) is spend just to move goods around the table before anything meaningful happens. Somebody might make a couple of bucks but I'm not sure it adds that much to the game. I'm sure you can do some tricks with goods and their pricing and try to either prevent your opponents from getting goods or get compensation in cash. Unfortunately this isn't too much fun nor interesting.

I don't like too much of the games where you can lock yourself out of the game if you do too many wrong decisions. In Container you can run out of many and goods. Actually you don't even need to run out of goods because other players don't have to buy anything from you because it would help you out of your misery. This is easy to prevent by always having enough cash but it always feels wrong to me in games if it is "too easy" to get yourself to this position. In Container it's easy enough and I don't like that.

After all these negative thoughs I must admit there were couple details in the game I liked. The blind auctioning the goods on the island is interesting choice in otherwise quite deterministic game. Although I'm not completely sure how informed the bids can be in the first rounds since nobody has much true info about how valuable the goods are to other players. Every player has different secret values for 5 types of goods. As an added annoyance you'll be losing the category that you have most goods. Actually I'm not sure if they can have that info even in midgame. This is because you really want to have most of your least valued good and then a little bit less of your two highest valued ones.

One more interesting thing in this auctioning of goods is the goverment subsidy for goods. The seller gets double the highest bid. Half of the money comes from buyer and half from the bank. This is actually the pretty much only way to get more money to the game. I liked this little detail in the game. At least it made sense both thematically and mechanically.

Overall the game just feels a little bit clunky in many ways. There is nothing seriously wrong but not too many things done right either. Four player game was ok but because there is so much player-to-player buying and selling I think game might be better with 5 players.

Update: I played a (half) game with 3 players. After that I just want to add that you need at least 4 players to play Container.

RATING: 8.6
Grandissimo gioco passato troppo sotto traccia e ora, disponibile solo a prezzi troppo elevati. Per molti aspetti mi ricorda un gioco Splotter. Ovvero gioco profondo con materiali ricercati ma al tempo stesso, per alcuni versi, pessimi per la poca praticità o chiarezza di alcuni dettagli tanto che il gioco sembra una versione test. Tra i difetti dei materiali trovo i colori delle merci, non chiari e non corrispondenti alle carte obiettivo, spazi dei porti non adeguati sia per ospitare navi che per mettere le carte dell'espansione, l'isola è decisamente brutta alla vista. A compensare parzialmente la cosa delle navi molto belle e particolari. Il gioco, da giocare esclusivamente con l'espansione e il mio voto tiene conto appunto di tale espansione che ne aumenta profondità ed interesse, è semplice e lineare ma al tempo stesso strategicamente complesso. E' un gioco di conti, di guadagni di domanda e offerta. Massima libertà per i giocatori di prezzare le merci in vendita, massima libertà in offerta per l'acquisto delle merci sull'isola e interessante la fase dei prestiti. Un piccolo gioiello che sarebbe opportuno riscoprire e magari rieditare.

RATING: 6
Middleweight build-an-economy game. Cubes at the destination island score differently for each player according to their secret mission cards, and these drive a lot of the action beforehand. Players can build machines to produce goods, and decide the prices to sell at. They're then dependent on other players to buy them and store at their warehouses, usually at a slightly higher price. Someone different from the warehouse owner then needs to pay for them and ship them to the island where they get blind-bid for as a lot, where it's relatively easy for each player to determine their potential end-game profit and hence value the lost (minus the wibble that you don't score whatever you have most of, which needs to be taken into account). Profit can be made at each step of the process, so there's a number of ways to play a strategy - producer, warehouser, shipper, combination of the lot. The problem is that the game gets samey. Turns are fast with only two actions, but it's typically just buying and selling of cubes from here to there, squeezing out profit here and there, trying to get the most valuable cubes (for you) to the island for purchase. The other problem is that if the players are "over-paying" for goods at the island, then that's the only strategy you can effectively use so as not to fall behind ekeing out dribs and drabs from producing / warehousing. It's different enough to keep for a while, but it's not a game that screams play me from the shelves.

RATING: 8
Tra i giochi della mia collezione, Container è quello che in assoluto si presenta peggio: quando lo proponi, viene notata per prima cosa la scarsa qualità delle plance e dei componenti in legno. A questo si aggiunge una pessima scelta dei colori, un vero e proprio incubo per i daltonici. Poi inizia la partita, e tutti scoprono che la grafica non è più importante: c'è un modello economico di domanda / offerta che pur non essendo macchinoso risulta estremamente realistico. A questo si aggiunge una profondissima interazione tra i giocatori: la farfalla che sbatte le ali in Cina provoca un uragano in America. Ogni mossa altera l'intero turno, proprio come il nostro sistema economico. Il rischio d'impresa è un fattore importantissimo, e vedersi pignorare i beni non è improbabile: quando la merce passa di mano, è necessario ritagliarsi un margine alto quel tanto che basta per rientrare nei costi, ma basso abbastanza da risultare conveniente e appetibile. Non è immediato percepire chi sta vincendo, e con gli obiettivi segreti il rischio Kingmaking è limitato. Inoltre, eliminare a fine gioco le merci più comuni, oltre a aggiungere realismo al gioco, rende i calcoli sugli avversari molto più complicati.
Non capita spesso di sentire dagli amici - "Lo giochiamo anche la prossima volta?" - prima che la partita corrente sia finita. Mai fermarsi alle apparenze: Container ha un brutto vestito, ma sotto nasconde un piccolo capolavoro.

RATING: 9
I absolutely adore this game. The first time I played it, we played with a really bizarre house rule of adjusting your prices throughout the game without taking an action. It was frustratingly annoying and killed the spirit of the game for me. You could "wheel and deal" and I felt like it was trying to make this game something that it's not.

After playing it a second time, we played it with the rules as written and I can't stop thinking about it. The money was tight, I took out loans, had to price things competitively and bidding was intense. You weren't sure if you were paying way too much or if that was even enough to influence the decision of the auctioneer. I can't wait to play this game more, but I'm unsure who I can get it to the table with. It's a solid 9, but it may become a 10 with subsequent plays. A lot to explore and discover and it's all player based with no help if you start to flounder. Brilliant!

RATING: 9
Wow. This is a very delicate game. Definitely not a game you want to play with new players. One player can very easily send themselves into total ruin and also completely wreck the economy in the game. The game is entirely player driven and needs an experienced group.

I play harsh games regularly - Age of Steam, Antiquity, 18xx - but this is one of the harshest I have seen. One game saw two players with loans that they could not pay the interest on resulting in the bank seizing all of their cash and assets. There is so little money in the game that every decision is critical and every dollar is important. This is definitely not an engine building game. Players must find a niche in the supply and demand network and make it profitable. It is very hard to do well, and is very harsh on mistakes. Sometimes just surviving can mean victory.

RATING: 9
It's an economic game unlike any other I've played. Players completely drive the economy and can asphyxiate it if they are not careful. 

It seems to be all about pricing, but it isn't. Setting the right prices at the right times is important, but there are a lot of very subtle dependencies that players need to take into account to be successful. Anticipating the trends and adapting is the key to success. 

It reminds me of modern art, but with a lot more depth and options. I've read about how players can compromise the game for everybody, if they play badly, but that hasn't been an issue in our games at all. 

This is truly a masterpiece of game design from the late Franz-Benno Delonge,  his magnum opus!

UPDATE: Increased the rating to 9.5 after yet another very rewarding game!

RATING: 6
Container is a game of supply and demand which is left entirely to the players to create and manage. The result is an extremely fragile system where one player can easily and completely unwittingly hand the game to another, simply by improper valuation or trying new tactics. I can see how Container could be a good game in a group of very experienced players, but the repetitiveness of the game and a play time easily double that listed on the box makes this exceedingly unlikely given its stiff competition for table time. What's more, I am not entirely convinced the game is not "broken" to the point that there is only one real viable strategy--that of shipping to the island.

That said, I love economic games and Container did grab me. Just not quite hard enough that I couldn't get away.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
I'm supposed to give the disclaimer here that I've only ever played this once, and online to boot. And since this game is obviously brilliant, no one is allowed to say negative things about it, as that would be proof of their unbrilliance in the face of the game's sheer brilliance. And we can't have that. Because who wants to appear unbrilliant? Nobody. 

I refuse to believe the nonsense that this game is an economic simulation. In no economy I'm aware of do you guess at pricing without knowing who your buyers are. Economies also don't use blind bidding, which might be correct here for a game mechanism, but it's wonky in a "pure" economic game. And nobody stays in business for very long if they insist that their buyers also buy unrequested, bundled goods of a completely different type. 

This game really advances  a buck at a time. Produce a good, someone stores it, someone ships it, and someone else buys the damn thing when made into a set that may or may not include something else they want. So the game is economic in that you have a bunch of middlemen who don't need to be there, boosting the cost for no real reason except to obfuscate the direct path to the consumer. 

Online we were able to track the bids and track the perceived valuations, and that would strike me as desirable in order to play this competently. It's not really possible to do that playing face-to-face, and so I'm hard-pressed to understand what playing it face-to-face does other than make it impossible (or socially awkward) to track the money flowing the way that online asynchronous play allows, where you can look at what this particular set of goods sold for last time it went to the island and price accordingly. I also felt completely powerless on many of my turns -- while I can concede that experience might mitigate that to a degree, the fact that you have to specialize means that your options are pretty limited on your turn, and too many times I felt like this economic machine was humming along, and when it got time for me to take my turn, I had to do my part to keep the machine going. A Geekbuddy once positioned this as a virtue, saying that the game is really about getting in and out of phase with the other players. And I believe that, but it's not like this happens every turn. To me this is a 20-turn game with maybe 5 interesting turns in it. The rest of the time you're just doing what you're supposed to do to prevent the game from locking up. Consequently, there is just no narrative arc to the game, no division between early-, mid-, and late-game periods. It's a game about enduring a dull, repetitive pattern in the hopes that you can recognize the handful of moves when the other players have created perfect conditions for you to earn an extra dollar. 

It kind of breaks my heart to read so many negative or indifferent comments on this game where the BGGer feels the need to self-flagellate and say that the problem most likely lies with them since the game is so well-received. This tendency to make oneself feel stupid has simply got to stop. This game is dry as fuck, drearily themed, and rewards the ability to spot a barely perceptible pattern difference in a game that otherwise lulls you to sleep with its wave-like rocking motion. I hereby grant everyone permission to state how they feel about Container without feeling the need to dock themselves a few IQ points.

RATING: 7
Container is an economic game with a heavy emphasis on logistics.  It can bog down in dry repetitive accounting excercises, but the interdependence of the players on each other makes for some amazing player interaction.

Players have factories of various colors.  These produce like colored Containers that other players can buy.  The producing player can set the price of each Container.  Purchased Containers are placed in Warehouses.  Other players can then bring their ships into port to buy Containers from the Warehouses.  Again players can set the price of these Conatiners.  Ships can then ship the Containers to the Island where they are sold via blind bid auction.  The player who is auctioning the Containers takes the high bid and an equal amount of cash from the bank.  In this way money flows in to the game.  

Containers are worth different amount of points to each player and the exact value is secret.  Also before counting score a player must discard the color of container they have the most of.

So the trick is that anything you produce you cannot buy.  Anything you store in your Warehouses you cannot buy.  So if you need a particular color of Container to go up for auction in the middle you can't channel it directly from your Factory to your Warehouse to your Ship to the Island.  You are dependant on other players taking up part of the chain.

Furthermore, players can set the price at which they will sell Containers.  So players need to track supply and demand as well as the prices other players have set in order to get as much money as possible for their goods.  Storage space is limited so if you are not moving containers you are going to feel stifled.

Timing is also important and having cash in hand for an important auction is a critical part of the game.

Like any economy players need to look for a need that the current system is failing to provide and fill it.  In a game players will fill various roles since no one player can effectively focus on Factories, Warehouses and shipping simultaneously.  This is where the game shines.  If everyone is trying to ship then the player with the most Warehouses will reap a windfall.  However Warehouses are useless without other players' Factories.  If no one is producing the game will grind to a halt, if one player is producing they can set their prices high and force others to pay it.  

Because eVerything is interrelated, players can break the game resulting in a failed economic state where players are poor, wealth isn't flowing into the game and piracy off Horn of Africa is the only option.  Since the piracy aspect is not part of the game, when poverty abounds the game drags.  There's nothing in the rules to save the players from themselves.

There are only a few actions a player can take on a turn while the game often lasts over two hours.  This can make it feel repetitive and tedious at times.

The saving grace is how tied together the players are.  They need each other in order to do well.  As a player it's a blast trying to create economic incentive for other players to do what you need them to do and still come out on top.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Container is an unique and interesting pure economics game. In fact, it is the a very distilled trading game. The rules are not complex and at the first glance seem to offer only little freedom to players: set prices for your own goods or purchase goods from other players. However, in this confined action space Container offers a lot of freedom and only very little constraints. Whereas in most Euro games each action has a certain effect and you know the outcome, setting prices in Container does not. You just do not know, what other players will pay for your goods.

Before each good is finally delivered to the island (where it counts as victory points), there are three stages of trading: sale from factory, sale from harbor and auction at the island. Each stage is unique: production has high fix costs with factories, but low variable costs; to supply goods at your harbor you have littel fix costs, but when setting the price you have to consider how much you paid to the producer; delivering the goods to the island has no fix costs, high variable costs, requires the most actions, and has an unknown return - but also the biggest return potential.

The economy is semi closed. By that I mean that players for the most part trade with each other (such that no money flows out from the economy), but there are a few mechanism where the amount of money in the economy changes: infrastructure investment is an outflow (also when you purchase goods from your own auction); at the island auctioners get twice the highest bid, whereas the doubling comes as an inflow from the bank. Typically for this closed economy, how one turn plays out depends on how much money is in the economy. Since the only inflow source (which can even turn into outflow if the auction offer is not accepted by the seller) is determined by auction, I believe the economy has potential to fail if players act foolishly.

I actually like this aspect. I have the impression that many modern Euros are very well designed, such that they are well balanced and is clearly defined how much points you do per action. This is not the case in Container. How much a good is worth pretty much depends on the conception of the players. I am really amazed how such a simple rule set creates such a complex game. 

I have only a few things to criticize, why Container is not one of my absolute top games. The gameplay is rather dry, which is also connected to the almost non-existent theme. In general I do not have any problem with a dry economic theme, but in Container the mechanisms are also not well thematically integrated: Why can you not buy from yourself; why is the auction offer doubled by the bank; why do containers magically fly from other players factories to your harbor; why do you want to get the containers to this island at all? (I know, without these mechanisms the game would simply not work.) While I like the ships and I am only little underwhelmed by the design of the factory  board, I do not like the color palette chosen for the game. Two of the ships are nearly indistinguishable; two of the container colors are also not too easy to separate. Last and certainly least: the graphic design of the island is simply abysmal.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.2
Quite an involved game. Many heart-breaking decisions. You are attempting to indirectly influence the play to get an advantage. High level of randomness/control-lessness, where you need to get your containers on the market by producing, selling and/or loading the cargo you need be delivered to your portion of the island. As a downside, the boats are well done but the colors are aweful, to a point where players got confused as to which one was theirs!?

RATING: 8
Clever tough weird economic game. We played 3 times in BGG.CON. I wish it worked for 2 players, not just 3-5. But very interesting and good. Auctions, price-setting, your best color doesn't count at the end ... neat tough choices.

PS: some people seem scared off from even trying Container, due to exaggerated warnings about how it has a "fragile economy" which new players will screw up and thus ruin the gameplay. In my experience, this warning is seriously exaggerated. I learned Container as a new player with other new players (including strangers) at a game con, literally with me reading the (quite short clear) rules aloud as we learned together, and our games went fine. So IMHO people should not let the "fragile economy, bad for new players, scary!" rhetoric mislead you into not trying Container if you get a chance.

RATING: 9
Update 07-2008: finally I got to play my third game of Container. It’s the third game which, according to self-imposed law, means I will rate it too. I was thinking along the line of a five, maybe a six for Container, as the first two games didn’t really flow that well and it took me a long time to get it to the table the first three times. But after yesterday’s play I’m convinced there is a brain burner (but without too much AP) that can be a [b]good[/b] game to the able minded. As far as I can tell (from the way I’m lost in all three games) there is much to learn for me about Container, and how to improve play. That conclusion, and the fact that I really enjoyed yesterday’s game, I say it’s a solid seven for now.
Container is like a good vintage wine: it has to ripe before it gets good. You have to give the game a few go's before you start to appreciate the fine aspects of the game. It's very unforgiving, in that respect it could even pass Caylus.

RATING: 6
Played one 4P game and one 5P game so far. In my very first game with 4P, we agreed to concede the game early as it seemed impossible to catch up with the player in the lead. Halfway through the game, players had begun undercutting prices at the harbour and factory store. This led to us being caught in a death spiral as our buying power steadily decreased and there was no way to inject enough money into the closed system.

That experience made me realise that Container is the closest I've gotten to a free market simulation in a board game (barring a few rules which don't make sense in a real-world scenario, like the restrictions on buying your own goods). Players have to constantly identify and exploit niche links in the economy to earn healthy profits, and these profits are then used to win auctions for containers. To keep this entire economic machinery afloat requires competent and involved players.

Despite the brilliance behind its concept, I can't quite get behind the need to constantly crunch numbers and keep tabs on prices paid. Combine that with its long playing time (roughly 3hrs with 5P), and Container holds more interest for me as an economic simulation as opposed to an actual game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Perhaps the greatest game about smuggling contraband ever made.  Create confusing paper trails, laundering everyone else's unmarked crates around.  You can't just produce goods and ship them to the island you have to elaborately lose the tail in order to get it to Mauritius.  

Notable mostly in just how unlike other economic games this feels. The majority of the game is groupthink, yet it isn't a stock game.  Everything you do is completely dependent on your fellow players, but the addition of this volatile nature to the game adds a real feeling of a supply/demand scenario.  Those that can't handle being at the mercy of other players should avoid this.

I felt caught up in the number of wonderful tough little decisions in a turn. Often though, I was just trying to figure out how to get past spinning my wheels.  Nicely interactive as you are dependent on others to get anywhere.  There's lots of money exchanging hands, so those that can't give anything away will have a hard time.

My rating will probably go up with more plays.

Great bits and art, despite the awkward center island.

UPDATE:It is still quite good with 5, but it becomes overly long.  Best to play this with 4.

RATING: 8
Valley Games, in my opinion, needs to place more emphasis on usability. Their design of the Die Macher cards causes confusion. And here, with Container, their color choices were atrocious: the orange and brown containers are too close; the light blue and lighter blue ships are too close; the playing mat colors don't match the ship colors; and a black container on the black ship is invisible.

That said, this is a superb game. We played 4-player last night and everyone loved it. There are many dynamic choices to be made through the game. I see this being played a lot.

Great design, somewhat flawed production. (The cargo ships are cool, though.)

Follow-up: newbies at this game insist on building up their factories and warehouse before starting to ship goods. It deflates the economics and takes a lot of the fun out of the game for me. And some people don't set their prices in the right ball park, or bid way too low or too high, which also saps at the game, in my view.

RATING: 7
The first time I played I was completely cut out of contention by not focusing on a niche. I finished dead last. The second time, having learned the basics, I made a lot of my money shipping, enough to hand me the win. The third time I finished in the middle of the pack. That third play was interesting, as price fixing and collusion lead to inflation.  This created an economic slump from which it took some time for everyone to recover. I wouldn't call it a depression, but it definitely slowed the game down.  As you might expect, the wealthiest player weathered the storm most effectively.

Container is opaque.  It is not easy to understand the full effects of your actions.  It is also very player-determined.  The economy can crash if the players cause it to.  More interesting than fun, it is a game I remain curious to better understand.

RATING: 4
One huuuuge flaw this has is the same problem that Modern Art has.  You can figure out what a shipment is worth to you in an auction. That is then doubled, and person who was the lucky recipient just shot way ahead.   The same thing happens in Modern Art - if you are playing with folks who don't realize that it's a game of splitting profits (and not making tiny profits on the sale), it throws the results into the wind.

I also found the game to be very plodding in flow.  It takes so long for containers to trickle from factories out to the island that things seemed to take a very long time.

RATING: 7
A weird game of supply and demand where you produce containers that only OTHER players may buy. When you buy from other players, they go into your Harbor Warehouse, where only OTHER players may buy them (loading their ships). When a ship sails to Foreign Island, it auctions the lot of containers. 

Initial game with 3 was OK, but 2nd play (with 5) was better if too long: more containers in the market, more competition and price-adjustment, more options since actions weren't as limited by what was available in only 2 player's boards. However, the game feels a bit repetitive: only 4 options each turn, without enough other stuff happening to keep it from getting really interesting. Not bad, but not great, either. Next time, would play with fewer containers.

RATING: 8
Impressively simple, yet complicated to play well. The game system is very minimalistic and leaves the players with a maximum of freedom to do what they want. 

Most euros tend to be optimization puzzles where players try to manipulate the system to their advantage. Not so Container, where it's just you, your fellow players and some rules-based constraints. Not everyone handles that well, so for many people, this comes across as a strange game. 

I liked it and was even more impressed by it. I've got the feeling I've only scratched the surface. But the fact that enjoying the game is so dependent on the players makes this game very vulnerable to breaking down and hence disappointing experiences. Therefore, only an 8 for now, but this may yet rise.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Difficult, [i]almost[/i] self-contained economic game. Money only leaves the system when players buy factories (produce containers) and warehouses (store containers produced by other players and purchased); it only enters the system when those goods (bought from another player's warehouse and shipped to the island) are auctioned off. The winning bid is matched by the bank.

I've heard horror stories about breaking the game by leeching too much money out of it; we might have ruined it in the opposite direction by allowing windfall profits (on the order of 300-400%) for island shipping. 

The whole thing reminds me of Chicago Express in that winning seems to be largely about incentive management and correctly estimating values to different players in order to do so. Because shipping takes so many separate stages (and vertical integration is illegal) it's impossible to go it alone -- you've got to get other players doing your dirty work. That said, actually estimating values correctly seems extraordinarily difficult due to the hidden prices for each player: I have no idea how to play well. In future I would like to play with open money to remove some of the guesswork.

In true Valley Games style, components are pretty awful. Actually, the money is OK and the resin ship pieces are nice and solid, only the colours don't match. I don't know what moron thought it was a good idea to have three of the commodities coloured in orange and two shades of brown, and I was shocked that the dreadful, washed-out artwork came from the usually good value Mike Doyle. None of this stuff is game-breaking, except in low light, but it does help in making the game feel even more dry and unpleasant.

A very interesting game.

RATING: 7
Origins was a pretty good experience for me in 2010; I had a chance to play a number of games for the first time. I spent almost all my time in the Board Room, which I can't recommend strongly enough to anyone who comes to Origins.

I played Container with three other guys who had never played it.

The mechanics of play were easy enough to grasp. I suspect there is some deep strategy underlying pricing your goods to manipulate the types of containers that end up shipped to the island, but to be honest I didn't grasp what it might be; I just priced my stuff "medium low" and the other players seemed willing enough to buy and ship my goods.

For shipping to the island and bidding on cargoes, I unintentionally stumbled onto the tactic of loading my ships with a combination of containers that would work well for myself, then landing on the island when the other players were low on cash. The resulting bids were low and I could then buy my own cargo cheap. I had no trouble ensuring that I had the most containers of my "cheap" type (thereby not counting them in the end) and getting a fairly large number of my first- and second-highest types. I ended up winning the game comfortably.

Play again? Yes, definitely. Because I am sure there is depth to this game that I just didn't pick up on in one play against equally-inexperienced opponents. I'll be blunt: I almost always lose complex games, so I was surprised that I came out on top. I want to play Container again so I can tell whether the win was actually good play or just dumb luck.

RATING: 7
[Valley Game 07 edition w/ plastic containers] {Shipping, economic; pick-up-delivery, blind-bidding, secret-goal; optimal 4p}  I usually like Franz-Benno Delonge's games.  They are usually thematic, interactive and medium-weight, which are very suitable for my friends.  Therefore, I decided to pre-order a copy of Container from Valley Games.

At first, I pre-order Container before the rule is published.  I thought it was a family game similar to other Franz-Denno's designs.  However, I was completely wrong.  Container is a unique game in my collection, in which the game situation is very opaque.  Inexperienced players cannot really understand what is happening; thus, they are vulnerable to be isolated. I witness cases that a player is held hopeless with no money inflow.

Container is a thematic medium-weight game, in which players indirectly trade between each other.  It is essentially a tactical game but the secret goal provides a very good strategic base.  The game progresses well (though a little bit slow) with a group of experienced players.  Decisions are meaningful in Container and sometimes players would like to do many things in a turn than allowed.  It needs a few games to understand the flow and potential of Container.

There are a few minuses.  Container may be quite processional for some players, in which player keep offering, buying and sailing.  Another problem is that Container may break if some players made a mistake or don't even know how to offer - then it may benefit another player too much that the fellow players cannot even match.  In addition, there are too many rule tweaks for my taste, which makes the system quite artificial (without a good integration with the theme).  (Players are complaining Container is fragile but I think it's more a problem of the players.)

I really don't like Mike Doyle's art style. The color tune is just too dark and saturated. It's very tried to look at them.  I don't know why current publisher still chooses brown and orange color together for the bits. It is so difficult to distinguish them.  The colors of ships are too close, too!

Umm, the pre-order experience is not good. Essen participants got the game earlier (why bother pre-ordering with such high shipping price).  Little communication and plastic containers are not delivered as promise. I won't consider pre-orders at Valley Games again.

RATING: 9
A simple free market game: produce, distribute, and sell. Within this simplicity, market psychology has free reign. Seems like an excellent basis for modification to teach basic Economics. Oh, and a really fun game! Even better than economic games that simulate market forces (cough, cough, Brass, Power Grid), Container actually has market forces. Modern Art on steroids, with much less chance of auctions getting out of hand.

I say simple. Some people, even economically sophisticated people, don't seem to "get" Container. How one can spend one's life in a capitalist country and not understand free markets I have trouble understanding. I don't always play the game well, but I don't feel lost in it.

[u]Basic Container economics:[/u]
* Building factories drains money to the bank.
* Building warehouses drains money to the bank.
* Buying product shifts money among the players.
* Auctions feed money into the selling player if he doesn't buy his own goods (because of the bank matching funds).

This game is [b]not[/b] about each person building the best economic engine! Container is about each person finding the best niche in a single economic engine and shaving money and points from that [b]one[/b] economy better than the other players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A great economic game no doubt, perhaps the greatest purely economic game. 'Container' not only offers wonderful decisions in terms of what to produce, how much to produce, how much to store, and what to buy--but also demands that players pay absolute attention to other players' decisions as they unfold, i.e. this game is highly meta-cognitive.  To this effect, it is better in my opinion to play 'Container' with open money.

Friends of mine who are economists tell me that 'Container' does a pretty good job of modeling authentic decisions that have to be made in the world of international business. Which is to say, 'Container' can be unforgiving. It is possible in this game to more or less wreck the economy for everyone.  Learning how to evaluate what other people think something is worth can be tricky. 

But to me, just as with other games that reward multiple plays, this is a plus, not a detraction.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've played Container twice. My tentative rating is very positive, but I have no idea if it's accurate. All I really know for sure is that I had a good time. I don't know why. I don't really know what happened. Goods were moving all over the place. I was trying to price them aggressively and fill up my island region. In my first game, I forgot the rule about disregarding your most plentiful good on the island -- and so I lost terribly.

All that said, this is a game for me. You have to pay attention to the other players, price things appropriately -- either up-selling or undercutting. You have to try to encourage the other players to do things that will bring value to your own portfolio. It's a mess, but it's fascinating.

What I love about Container is that the economy is entirely player-drivin. Yet unlike so many games that have player-driven economies, there is a fragility that feels as though the training wheels are off. Complaints about the game grinding to a halt are overblown. So long as everyone understands the consequences of pricing, there is no reason to assume that Container is, for example, "only for serious players." Instead, I feel as though the game includes a built-in sense of exhilaration. Perceiving that the game economy could fall apart, and that everything is under your control, is a bit like driving your car too fast: dangerous but exciting. Don't get me wrong, Container is no sports car, but at the same time it doesn't care if you spin out after hitting the accelerator too hard.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Container is a pure economic game. Player A produces goods and sells them to Player B. Player B then sells those goods to Player C (or back to Player A). Player C then auctions those goods to the highest bidder (possibly including himself). The seller always gets to set the price, so there is a very organic free market economy system. Unfortunately in my one play the market was always pretty fixed, so that feature didn't really get to shine. The goods are worth variable amounts to each player, but the scoring system is set up so the differences aren't that extreme.

It's not a bad euro. I don't know if the purely economic game play would be enough to keep my attention for long, but I lost badly the first time so I'd like to play it again now that I understand the strategies better.

RATING: 5
I am not really sure where this game is going. It might go up but it might also go down in my rating. I am definitely willing to give it a chance. The problems:

- Start player disadvantage (in the first round of play no player bought any goods from the industrial area of another player in order to place them in their own harbor area, thus in the second round the start player had no chance of docking his ship anywhere to pick up goods. 
- Fall behind loser-problem - when you screwed up early during the game you're out of the rest of the game. No matter what you'll do there is no chance to take influence in a way that would give you a chance to come back into the game. Personally I detest floating through the remaining 2 hours of a game knowing that. 
- Game length is way above the 60-90 minutes (it's rather 3 to 3 1/2 hours) 
- Investing in machines seems rather unprofitable
- It seems most of the time you don't find a market full of lucrative deals but you rather have to buy from the player to your right (because he or she has just produced and goods have not been bought so far) 

What I liked about the game was its dual entrepreneurship you have to think yourself into. On one side you are the industrialist trying to sell your goods, on the other hand you are a shipping company or rather the port supervisor trying to get the goods on their way. The scoring system balances itself nicely. By removing the kind of goods you collected the most from you need to also get stuff you don't need at all. 

To sum it up:

If all of the above Cons turn out to be mistakes of the game rather than mistakes of the players rating will go down. In the other case it'll move up

RATING: 8
Rated an 8 after my first two plays. It's likely to go up if I keep liking it as I play more. What I like about it so far is the way players have to react well to what others are doing. It takes at least one game to get to that point, however--and I'm sure I'll keep discovering more things to consider. 

In my second play, I focused mostly on pick-up-and-deliver to make my money because everybody was producing in such abundance. It worked, I hardly produced at all and still had money to be competitive. Whereas in the first game I played, if I had tried the same strategy, I would have had very little money.

The game creates a sort of co-dependency for all players to make money, and spend money for the economic cycle to continue. And there's no other game I have that simulates that sort of economic fragility so well as this. For that, I'll probably keep it in my collection forever.

A true gem for any economic gamer.

RATING: 7.1
Many people find this game to be unbalanced, and become frustrated with the lengthy gameplay and the apparent superiority (and potential kingmaking) of the shipping strategy.

As JC has noted elsewhere, building a stronger understanding as a group of the relative value of each step of the process can help mitigate the groupthink and balance the table.  We've found that AP prone players (like myself) can really bog this down if they try to calculate every possible move and the exact value of every action (and auction) for themselves.

Our current solution?  Speed Container.  We've taken to playing with a 20-second timer for all moves.  It makes the game fast, fluid, and fun.  I'm not sure clearclaw would approve, but it solves our immediate issue with the length and pace of the game.  

Another game that needs the 20-second timer treatment?  [gameid=47].

RATING: 7
What an odd game! It's a standard theme of players producing and shipping goods on boats, but the quirk is that you can't just ship the goods that you produce. You need other players to voluntarily choose to purchase and process the goods first, and then you can't ship the goods that you've purchased and processed. So the entire game is extremely interactive as it revolves around passing these goods back-and-forth as you essentially work together to get them from production to shipping. What I liked about Container is that it allowed players to specialize, really and truly specialize. Unlike many games that give the appearance of allowing specialization, while rewarding and encouraging a broad approach (e.g., Agricola, Endeavor), Container not only permitted but also didn't attempt to dissuade players from specializing in a particular facet of the supply chain, whether that be producing, processing, or shipping goods. What I didn't like about Container is that it seemed like a very fragile system that could cease to function relatively easily if the players didn't play "correctly" to make it work. It seems as if the game could easily break if the players don't essentially work together to keep the system running smoothly. It's definitely a game I would need to play a few more times to better grasp how it all fits together.

RATING: 7.5
[thing=26990][/thing] is a game great game, but one I will usually avoid play with someone that hasn't played it before. This happens because the blind bidding for goods can make a game end basically in one round. If someone overbid by too much, the player will get not only this amount, but also twice as much, since the bank always pays the same amount for the player.

Also, the market is determined by the players. This can be really enjoyable, but if one or more players want to "game" it, they will be able. This could mean almost breaking the game: if the amount payed for goods in the island isn't worth the trouble to send ships and buy them, the game can come into a halt - no one wants to lower the prices, and no one wants to buy them.

[thing=26990][/thing] is a player-dependant game. This means both a good thing and a dangerous thing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Pretty brilliant and elegant/slick design. The fun factor of the gameplay [i]maybe[/i] lags a bit behind the quality of the design but I'm not sure yet.

Basically, players produce containers (wooden bits), and want to finish the game owning a collection of containers that maximizes their personal (and secret) scoring equation, which is slightly convoluted. (The five colors of containers are valued differently by each player, with a possible bonus for having at least one of each color, and with a Knizian twist of having to trash the color you have the most of.) However, a container must be sold three times between production and scoring, and you can't sell to yourself.

Every time you buy something, your inclination is probably to resell it at a higher price. But clearly there's a limit to what that price can be. That, I would expect, would put it in the extortion/haggling category of games that I don't really like. However, Container's rules never call for negotiation. You set your prices and people take it or leave it (you can adjust later if you want). The third sale is a blind bid auction, and if the seller doesn't like any offers he can buy his own stuff (which can be devastating as the bank matches funds in the auction if you sell to someone aside from yourself).

A given turn can be blazingly fast. You only get two actions and aside from the auctions they are all very simple. At first I thought this was too insubstantial and I couldn't keep a train of thought. I also wondered, at first, where the game could possibly be, as it seemed like players were just acting out an economic simulation with few strategic choices. I still have some doubts about this, but gradually I did start to realize that different approaches were possible, and that the auctions needed to be carefully considered, in regard to how valuable an offer is to each player, whether you want to be the one selling something, and whether the players you hope to sell to will even have any money at the time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Perhaps the most open gaming platform in the Euro field, the players create the game using simple rules to raise a common economy with interdependencies that takes a patient stance to exploit. The use of hidden information - each player has hidden, differing values for each color container circulating the economy - adds a layer of deduction that gives it the potential of bluff & a surprise reveal.

I've had good & bad plays & I've become ambivalent on whether to play. It's player-driven genes can birth an odd duck here & there, but it's not just that. More that I may project onto it more than it is. Or it may be it good play can be reduced to a rule of thumb set of tactics driven by calculation that exposes, but doesn't accommodate, the irrationality that drives economies.

RATING: 10
Easily my favorite game - it's a shame that I realized this too late to buy it for cheaper.

Of the handful of games I've played, no game does a better job simulating real-world microeconomic and macroeconomic principles with such simple mechanics. Business owners in small, primarily closed economies must support one another, and there must be very little selfish, cutthroat activity. As the economy grows, companies have increased cash flow, and you can afford occasional cutthroat or selfish acts.

Similarly, the earliest turns in Container should be quite civil and friendly. Once more money has been added to the economy, players can begin to act more out of self-interest. (Of course, none of this applies if you're content with a game (economy) where everyone is strapped for cash and has to take out multiple loans. But I prefer games (economies) where debt is minimal and cash flow is strong!)

In your earliest turns in Container, I've found it key to do two things:

1. Don't go too crazy buying additional machines and warehouses (this causes money to exit the closed economy, and you can quickly halve the amount of cash in play)
2. In the earlier auctions, if you actually want a shipment, bid what you think it's worth. Don't lowball. If I'm interested, I typically start with a bid of $2.00-3.00 per container, understanding that the seller paid at least $2 per container to load up the ship. The results of these auctions will set the trajectory for the game's economy for the rest of the game.

The rules regarding scoring island containers are genius, and make for some very surprising results. Very little luck is included in this game, but these rules produce a level of unpredictability that keep everyone interested throughout the entire game.

RATING: 6
Container really demands repeated plays to get the most enjoyment out of the game's subtle and somewhat fragile mechanics. It reminds me of Modern Art in many ways, and I think whoever likes that game will probably like Container as well. Exquisitely sensitive to groupthink. Markets can never be cornered permanently, so the key to profit is being "in the right place at the right time", having the most desirable service (whether it be factory, warehouse, or boat goods) at a particular moment. The game is basically an iteration of this theme.

RATING: 9
Pure economics, no luck at all(...I guess there is a VERY little bit).  There are two interesting bits to this game.  First is that players aren't able to specialize, they'll need to participate in all parts of the economy to make money.  Second is that the game can be 'broken' if players play poorly.

I've played the game with all 5 players, and 4 of them being new and we didn't 'break' the game so I imagine that this is very hard to do.

Seems that the most money is made selling goods at auction though, and that the rest of the game is just making sure there are enough goods to sell them.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The last time I played a game that wowed me right off the bat, it was Wabash Cannonball.  Container follows in those footsteps, and it's not a coincidence that the games are both made up of a closed economic system, with extra money only coming in to the games at determined times.  It's also no coincidence that the games are both perfect information games (well, with Container, it's near-perfect, since no one is ever entirely sure which containers the other players are hoping to score), and that the way the game plays out is entirely dependent on the choices that players make during the game.  Like Wabash Cannonball, Container is also a relatively short game for its depth.  It has a delicate (or fragile, depending on how you look at it) economic system which is prone to "breaking" when players don't understand the consequences of overbidding, but the same can be said of Modern Art, and besides, in both games, it's more a characteristic of the game to manage, not a problem with the system itself.  In addition, all of the games I've played of Container played out VERY differently, which impresses me, as well.  In short, I really enjoyed this game, and I can see myself wanting to play it often.

5 plays: 4 players
3 plays: 5 players

RATING: 7
Good game; will take longer than the advertised box time, especially on the first few plays. Moves fairly smoothly, and each choice is a hard one. Will take a few plays to get a grip on how this game works: you have very little direct control over gathering income, but more direct control over investing your income; as such, you will find directing the flow of your game difficult because it's not at first clear where the cost/benefit points are nor how to regulate your income/expenses properly. Also, it appears to be one of those games where it's relatively important to have everyone at the table have a roughly similar level of experience with the game: newbies, expect to get crushed by those teaching you for a game or two. (Also, keep a very close eye on the ending conditions for the game; it can sneak up on you very quickly.)

RATING: 8
This a fascinating, albeit somewhat fragile game design. It's not a philosophical frailty like Archipelago where players can tank the game, but rather a skill cap frailty. In other words, if everyone at the table isn't playing at a near exact skill level, the game can get chaotic in a hurry. The game runs on a closed economy, so over-development or bad investments by one player can have serious consequences for everyone else at the table. In many ways, that is just another unique and challenging environment to play around in and that's fine. It's the exact sort of thing you look for in a sandbox game like Container.

The problem is mainly in the auction phase, as this is the primary way that new money enters the game. If someone at the table is baffled by the game's economics or they start to frequently overvalue shipments, the game can quickly go off the rails with either king-making money swaps or completely random explosions of profit. Both are somewhat unsatisfying outcomes and the threat of their occurrence stops me from rating this one too high.

In a solid group, where everyone understands the repercussions of their actions however, the game absolutely shines. It is astounding how much depth there is in such an easy rule set. This is an extremely meaty game, but the rules can easily be condensed down into a page and a half. The other thing I like about it is that while skilled play is important, competence never seems to lead to predictability. There are simply too many layers in the supply chain for most people to calculate exact profits. Some folks will be able to do so, of course. They will be tough to beat. Still, this is a game that rewards skill, so that is to be both expected and celebrated.

Just make sure everyone levels up their play at the same pace and you won't have problem. It's shame this out of print, because I think it belongs on the shelf of every serious heavy gamer.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Interesting game, I like the feeling of pricing your wares.

UPDATE: There's a nice feeling of an economic engine in this one, and a very nice dynamic in undercutting pricing, spotting the hot commodities, and figuring out what each player wants (and mixing the shipments accordingly). Also, while shipping may initially seem overvalued compared to everything else, it does take 4 actions, versus the high potential for other profit-making actions to only require 1 action.

There can be odd issues with player order, if production/warehousing is on a downturn, since you may be stuck with nothing to do. However, such a situation probably goes away once everyone understands the profit possible in all the various actions. Underproducing can also slow the game down.

And this leads to the fragility of the economic engine. Players can screw themselves, badly, and take themselves out of the running even worse than by simple elimination. Think "ever-growing black hole of debt". (Well, not really worse, but it feels worse.) Perhaps unlimited loans would be an amusing variation, for expert players only, to allow extremely risky strategies. 

But beyond that, although it's never happened in my games, collusion can really screw the game up, if you exploit the shipping payout. If you play optimally, the collusion shouldn't last more than one turn, but this depends entirely on the players. It's never come up, but the fact that this problem can crop up is like a distraction... maybe it'll go away if I play a few more times.

If the play time goes down, it might rate a 7 for me.
